The Three Necessary Elements of Pleasure and Pain
Enumeration of three necessary elements for pleasure, with clarification and reference to Avicenna.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three essential components of pleasure and pain according to classical Islamic philosophy, and why attainment is distinct from mere imagination.

Core Idea
Pleasure requires actual attainment, not just imagination.

Before delving into the finer points of pleasure and pain, the commentator establishes the fundamental components that must be present. These are not simply physical reactions but involve perception, a judgment of agreeableness, and crucially, the actual attainment of the object. This lesson unpacks these three elements and clarifies a common confusion between imagining pleasure and experiencing it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three necessary elements
Read the passage and list the three things required for pleasure and pain: perception, the object being agreeable/disagreeable in belief, and attainment of that object.
The first two are considered obvious by the author.
2
Understand why attainment is necessary
The author argues that without attainment, one only imagines pleasure, which is not the same as actual pleasure. Compare this to imagining sweetness versus tasting it.
This refutes the idea that mere imagination constitutes pleasure.
3
Examine the clarification of 'from himself'
The commentator explains that 'from himself' means 'in himself', citing Quran 35:40. Understand this as a linguistic clarification.
4
Review Ibn Sina's different statements
The passage attributes the definition 'pleasure is perception of the agreeable' to Ibn Sina, but notes that he presented various nuances in different works. Consider how these nuances align with the three elements.
This shows that the definition is not monolithic even within a single philosopher's work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( إدراك الملائم إلخ لا خفاء في أنه لا بد في اللذة والألم من أمور ثلاثة : الإدراك وكون المدرك ملائماً أو منافراً في اعتقاده، ونيل نفس ذلك المدرك، أما الأولان فظاهران إذ بانتفائهما تنتفي اللذة والألم، وأما الثالث فلأنه إذا لم يكن نيل هناك بل اللذة كان رضاك تخيل اللذة والألم لا يتصفهما كما في تخيل الحلاوة والمرارة ولا يختلجن في ذهنك التباس تخيل قوله : ( يدركهما من نفسه الأقرب أن من بمعنى في كما في قوله تعالى أروني ماذا خلقوا من الأرض﴾ [فاطر:٤٠] . قوله : ( وقيل اللذة إدراك الملائم قائله ابن سينا فإنه ذكر في الفصل الأخير من المقالة الثامنة من إلهيات الشفاء أن اللذة ليست إدراك الملائم من حيث هو ملائم وذكر أيضاً في فصل من المقالة التاسعة أن القوى مشتركة في أن شعورها بموافقها وملائمها هو الخير واللذة الخاصة وذكر في الأدوية القلبية أن اللذة إدراك لحصول الكمال الخاص بالقوة المدركة، إلا أنه المعاد
His saying: (Perception of the agreeable, etc.) It is not hidden that for pleasure and pain there must be three things: perception, the perceived being agreeable or disagreeable in his belief, and attainment of that perceived itself. As for the first two, they are obvious, for if they are absent, pleasure and pain cease to exist. As for the third, because if there is no attainment, rather pleasure, your satisfaction [would be] imagination of pleasure and pain, which does not characterize them, just as in the imagination of sweetness and bitterness, and let not the confusion of imagination be mixed in your mind. His saying: (perceives them from himself) — the closest is that 'from' is in the sense of 'in', as in His saying Almighty: 'Show Me what they have created in the earth' [Fatir:40]. His saying: (And it was said: Pleasure is the perception of the agreeable) — its proponent is Ibn Sina, for he mentioned in the last chapter of the eighth article of the Theology of the Healing that pleasure is not the perception of the agreeable insofar as it is agreeable, and he also mentioned in a chapter of the ninth article that the faculties share in that their awareness of what is suitable and agreeable is the good and the specific pleasure, and he mentioned in The Cardiac Medicines that pleasure is the perception of the attainment of the perfection specific to the perceiving faculty, except that it is the final end.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of pleasure as 'perception of the agreeable'. It begins by stating that pleasure and pain require three things: (1) perception, (2) the perceived being agreeable or disagreeable in one's belief, and (3) attainment (nīl) of that perceived. The first two are obvious. The third is necessary because without attainment, one only imagines pleasure, which does not actually constitute it, just as imagining sweetness does not make something sweet. The commentary then clarifies the phrase 'perceives them from himself' as 'in himself', citing Quran 35:40. Finally, it attributes the definition 'pleasure is perception of the agreeable' to Ibn Sina, noting that in his works he presented different nuances: in the Theology of the Healing he denied that pleasure is mere perception of the agreeable; in another chapter he stated that awareness of the suitable is the good and specific pleasure; and in The Cardiac Medicines he defined pleasure as perception of attaining the perfection specific to the perceiving faculty. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating continuation on a later page.

إدراك الملائم
النيل
ابن سينا
القوة المدركة
الكمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that imagining pleasure or pain is sufficient to experience them.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that attainment is necessary; without it, one only has imagination, which does not characterize actual pleasure or pain.

Checkpoint

A) Perception
B) The perceived being agreeable in belief
C) The act of will
D) Attainment of the perceived

Key Takeaway
Pleasure and pain require perception, a judgment of agreeableness/disagreeableness, and actual attainment; mere imagination is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Al-Razi's Objection: Why Health Cannot Be a Psychological Quality
Al-Razi's objection with analysis of disease types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that health cannot be a psychological quality because its opposite (disease) belongs to other Aristotelian categories.

Core Idea
If disease isn't psychological, then health can't be either.

Al-Razi challenges the classification of health as a psychological quality by examining its opposite, disease. Since physicians agree that disease types fall under sensible qualities, relation, or passion—not psychological—he argues health must also be excluded. This passage walks through the detailed analysis of bad temperament to show how disease is categorized.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow of al-Razi's argument. Then read the translation carefully.
Notice that the passage begins with 'وأورد الإمام الرازي' indicating an objection.
2
Identify the three disease types
Al-Razi lists three types of disease agreed upon by physicians: bad temperament, bad composition, and discontinuity of connection.
These are the 'أجناس' of disease.
3
Analyze the breakdown of bad temperament
Bad temperament is further analyzed into three aspects: the quality itself, its being strange/repugnant, and the body's being described by it.
Each aspect belongs to a different category.
4
Map aspects to Aristotelian categories
The quality (e.g., heat) is a sensible quality; its being strange is a relative (مضاف); the body's description is a passion (انفعال).
None are psychological.
5
Understand the implication
Since disease (the opposite of health) is not psychological, health cannot be psychological either, as opposites share the same category.
This refutes the original classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد الإمام الرازي على جعلها أي جعل الصحة من الحالة والملكة أي من الكيفيات ( الكيفيات النفسانية سؤالاً هو أنَّ مقابلها المرض وليس المرض (منها) أي من النفسانية، فلا تكون الصحة أيضاً منها وإنما قلنا في المرض ليس منها (إذا جناسه) أي أنواعه المندرجة تحته باتفاق الأطباء ثلاثة (سوء المزاج وسوء التركيب وتفرق الاتصال، وهي ) أي هذه الأمور المذكورة ( إما من الكيفيات ( المحسوسة أو من مقولة (الوضع أو عدم فإِنَّ سوء المزاج الذي هو مرض، إنما يحصل إذا صار إحدى الكيفيات الأربع أزيد أو أنقص، مما ينبغي بحيث لا تبقى الأفعال سليمة، فهناك أمور ثلاثة : تلك الكيفية وكونها غريبة منافرة واتصاف البدن بها، فإن جعل المرض الذي هو سوء المزاج عبارة عن تلك الكيفية كان يقال: الحمى هي الحرارة الغريبة كان من الكيفيات المحسوسة، وإن جعل عبارة عن كون تلك الكيفية غريبة منافرة كان من باب المضاف وإن جعل عبارة عن اتصاف البدن بها كان من قبيل الانفعال، واقتصر المصنف من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة على الأول، فلذلك حكم بأن تلك
And Imam al-Razi raised an objection against making health (i.e., making health one of the states and faculties) among the psychological qualities. He said: Its opposite is disease, and disease is not among them (i.e., psychological qualities), so health also is not among them. We only say disease is not among them because its kinds—which, by agreement of physicians, are three: bad temperament, bad composition, and discontinuity of connection—these mentioned things are either from sensible qualities or from the category of position or non-being. For bad temperament, which is disease, only occurs when one of the four qualities becomes more or less than it should, such that actions are not sound. So there are three things: that quality, its being strange and repugnant, and the body's being described by it. If disease, which is bad temperament, is made to be that quality, then it would be said: fever is the strange heat, which would be from sensible qualities. If it is made to be that the quality is strange and repugnant, then it would be from the category of the relative. If it is made to be the body's being described by it, then it would be from the category of passion. And the author limited himself from these three divisions to the first, therefore he judged that that...
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents al-Razi's objection to classifying health as a psychological quality (كيفية نفسانية). Al-Razi argues that since disease, the opposite of health, is not a psychological quality, health cannot be one either. He then supports this by showing that disease types—bad temperament, bad composition, and discontinuity—fall under other categories: sensible qualities, the category of position, or non-being. He analyzes bad temperament in detail: it involves a quality (e.g., heat), its being strange, and the body's description by it. Each of these belongs to a different Aristotelian category. The author of the commentary (al-Ījī) notes that the original author (al-Razi?) limited himself to the first division, which is why he ruled that disease is from sensible qualities.

سوء المزاج
سوء التركيب
تفرق الاتصال
المضاف
الانفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Health and disease are both psychological qualities because they relate to the soul.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that disease involves physical qualities (heat, composition) and relations, not psychological states. Therefore health, as its opposite, must also be non-psychological.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what is the basis for concluding that health is not a psychological quality?
A) Health is not a quality at all
B) Disease, its opposite, is not a psychological quality
C) Physicians classify disease differently
D) Health is a state, not a faculty

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's objection shows that classification of qualities must be consistent with their opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi argues that opposites belong to the same category. If you claim to be a believer, yet indulge in sins that contradict faith, are you comfortable with that opposition? What would it take for your deeds to be in the same 'category' as your profession of faith?
?
The passage shows that a condition like disease can be analyzed into multiple aspects: the quality, its being foreign, and your being described by it. Think of a persistent spiritual flaw in your life. Can you identify which of these aspects you have been ignoring? Will you now address all three, not just the surface symptom?
?
Al-Razi's objection forces consistency in classification. Where in your life are you inconsistent? Do you hold others to a standard you do not apply to yourself? Will you commit to aligning your inner state with your outward actions, starting today?

Definition of Health and Resolution of Circularity
Defines health as a disposition and resolves the circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of health as a disposition avoids circularity by distinguishing between sensible health of actions and non-sensible bodily health.

Core Idea
Health is defined by actions, not by itself, breaking the loop.

This passage from Sanusi's work tackles a fundamental logical objection to defining health. Critics argued that defining health as 'soundness' is circular. Sanusi clarifies that the term 'health' in bodily health refers to the health of actions, which is perceptible. This clever move preserves the definition without circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: definition, objection, response.
The passage is concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the definition of health
The passage begins by stating that health is a disposition (ملكة) or state (حالة). Mark this as the core definition.
This is a standard kalam definition.
3
Understand the circularity objection
The objection claims that 'sound' is defined as 'healthy', so defining health by soundness is circular. Try to see why this seems problematic.
Circularity would make the definition meaningless.
4
Examine the resolution
Sanusi responds by distinguishing two senses of health: bodily health (non-sensible) and health of actions (sensible). The definition uses the sensible to define the non-sensible, which is valid because the sensible is clearer.
This avoids defining a thing by itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الخامس - المقصد الثاني : الصحة هي ملكة أو حالة. الثالث : السليم هو الصحيح فالتعريف دوري أي تحديد للشيء بنفسه حيث عرف الصحة بالصحة. قلنا : السلامة المأخوذة في تعريف صحة البدن هو صحة الأفعال. والصحة في الأفعال محسوسة معلومة بمعاونة الحس. والصحة في البدن غير محسوسة فعرف غير المحسوس بالمحسوس لكونه أجلى فلا إشكال.
The fifth type - second purpose: Health is a disposition or state. Third: The sound is the healthy, so the definition is circular, i.e., defining a thing by itself, since health is defined by health. We say: The safety taken in the definition of the health of the body is the health of actions. And health in actions is sensible, known with the aid of sense. And health in the body is non-sensible, so the non-sensible was defined by the sensible because it is clearer. So there is no problem.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage defines health and addresses a potential circularity objection. It begins by stating the definition: health is a disposition or state. Then it presents an objection: since 'healthy' is defined as 'sound', and 'sound' means 'healthy', the definition is circular—defining health by itself. The response clarifies that the term 'safety' in the definition of bodily health refers to the health of actions, which is perceptible by the senses. Bodily health itself is not directly perceptible, but it is defined by the sensible health of actions because the sensible is more evident. Therefore, the definition is not circular; it defines the non-sensible by the sensible.

Key Terms
صحة
ملكة
حالة
دور
سلامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That defining health by health is necessarily circular.
Correction: The definition is not circular because it defines non-sensible bodily health by the sensible health of actions, which are distinct.

Checkpoint
How is the circularity objection resolved in this passage?
A) By rejecting the definition entirely.
B) By redefining health in terms of actions which are sensible.
C) By appealing to divine revelation.
D) By using a different term for health.

Key Takeaway
Health is defined as a disposition, and the apparent circularity is resolved by distinguishing between the non-sensible health of the body and the sensible health of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you tend to define your spiritual health by inward feelings or by outward actions? Which is more clear and reliable to you? How does this passage's distinction challenge you to rely on the sensible signs of faith?
?
Think of a concept you often use but have never defined clearly. Is your understanding of it circular—defined by itself without reference to anything observable? How can you break that cycle by linking it to something sensible, as this lesson does with health?
?
When you examine yourself, do you ever misdiagnose your condition because you define health only by absence of harm, ignoring the active health of your deeds? What would change if you measured your health by your actions instead of by your feelings?

Definition of Illness
Defines illness as a state causing unsound actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic philosophers defined illness in relation to health and its scope in animals and plants.

Core Idea
Illness is defined as the contrary of health causing unsound actions.

This passage begins by stating that illness is the opposite of health. The author then defines illness as a state or disposition from which unsound actions proceed. This definition is general, covering all types of illnesses, but can be specified. Let's examine this definition closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the definition of illness.
2
Identify the definition
Find the exact wording of the definition: illness is a state or disposition causing unsound actions.
The term 'ma'ūfa' means disordered.
3
Consider the scope
The definition applies to animals and plants, but can be specified to humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمرض خلاف الصحة ومقابلها. فهي حالة أو ملكة يصدر بها الأفعال عن الموضوع لها غير سليمة بل مأوفة. وهذا يعم أنواع الأمراض في الحيوانات والنباتات وقد يخص على قياس ما تقدم في الصحة بالحيوان أو بالإنسان. وأنت خبير بما يرد على هذا الحد مما ذكره الإمام من عدم اندراج المرض في الكيفيات النفسانية وبأن المرض على هذا الحد يقابل الصحة تقابل التضاد. وفي القانون أن المرض هيئة مضادة للصحة. وفي الفصل الثاني من سابعة قاطيغورياس الشفاء مثل ذلك. وفي الفصل الثالث من هذه المقالة السابعة أن المرض من حيث هو مرض بالحقيقة عدمي لست أقول من حيث هو مزاج أو ألم. وهذا يدل على أن التقابل بينهما تقابل العدم والملكة. وفي المباحث المشرقية: لا مناقضة بين كلاميه إذ في وقت المرض أمر: أحدهما عدم الأمر الذي كان مبدأ للأفعال السليمة، وثانيهما مبدأ للأفعال المأوفة. فإن سمي الأول مرضاً كان التقابل تقابل العدم والملكة، وإن جعل الثاني مرضاً فالتقابل من قبيل التضاد.
Illness is the opposite of health and its contrary. It is a state or disposition by which actions issue from the subject unsound rather disordered. This applies to all types of illnesses in animals and plants, and may be specified analogously to what preceded in health to animal or human. And you are expert in what occurs to this definition from what the Imam mentioned: that illness is not included among psychological qualities, and that on this definition illness opposes health by the opposition of contrariety. In the Canon [of Medicine] illness is a form contrary to health. In the second chapter of the seventh [book] of the Categories of al-Shifa' the same. In the third chapter of this seventh article: illness, inasmuch as it is illness in reality, is privative—I do not mean inasmuch as it is temperament or pain. This indicates that the opposition between them is the opposition of privation and possession. And in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: there is no contradiction between his two statements, since at the time of illness there are two matters: one of them is the absence of the thing which was the principle of sound actions, and the second is the principle of unsound actions. If the first is called illness, the opposition is that of privation and possession; if the second is made illness, the opposition is of the type of contrariety.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage defines illness and tackles the question of how it relates to health. It begins by stating illness as the opposite and contrary of health, defined as a state or disposition causing unsound actions. The definition generalizes to animals and plants but can be specified. The passage notes an objection from the Imam: on this definition, illness would be a psychological quality, but it is not; also, it would oppose health by contrariety. Reference is made to the Canon and al-Shifa where illness is said to be contrary to health. However, in al-Shifa's third chapter, illness is said to be privative in reality, implying privation opposition. The resolution from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya reconciles: there are two aspects—the absence of the principle of sound actions and the presence of a principle of unsound actions. If illness is identified with the absence, the opposition is privation; if with the positive principle, it is contrariety. So no true contradiction.

Key Terms
مرض
صحة
أفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The absence of health
B) A state or disposition causing unsound actions
C) A pain in the body
D) A psychological quality

Key Takeaway
Illness is defined as a state or disposition that produces unsound actions, opposite to health.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are physically ill, do you notice how your actions—your prayers, your patience, your treatment of others—become unsound? What one action will you strive to keep sound despite your next illness?
?
This lesson defines illness as a state that produces unsound actions. Is there a 'disposition' in your heart—like pride, envy, or heedlessness—that acts like an illness, causing your actions to be disordered? What concrete step will you take today to seek its cure?

Does Illness Oppose Health by Privation or Contrariety?
Examines whether illness opposes health by privation or contrariety and resolves the ambiguity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether the opposition between health and illness is one of privation or contrariety and the reconciliation offered by al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya.

Core Idea
Illness has two aspects: privation of health and positive disorder.

The previous lesson defined illness. Now we delve into how illness relates to health: are they opposites like hot and cold (contrariety) or like sight and blindness (privation)? The passage presents objections, references from medical and philosophical works, and a resolution that clarifies there is no contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the objection from the Imam.
2
Identify the two views
Some sources say illness is contrary to health; others say it is privative. Find the quotes.
3
Analyze the resolution
The passage resolves by distinguishing two aspects at the time of illness: absence of sound action principle and presence of unsound action principle.
This reconciles the apparently conflicting statements.

No Intermediate Between Health and Illness
Proves no intermediate between health and illness via action analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why there is no intermediate state between health and illness based on the logical principles of affirmation and negation, and how the objection about partial health/illness is resolved.

Core Idea
Health and illness are contradictory opposites, not gradations with a middle ground.

This lesson examines a key theological argument about health and illness. The passage shows that when we define them correctly, there can be no intermediate state. An objection raises the possibility of mixed actions, but the author clarifies that universality applies per action, not collectively. Understanding this logic prevents confusion about categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Focus on the definitions of health and illness given in the text.
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the objection and answer
Look for where the text says 'قيل' (it was said) to find the objection. Then find the answer starting with 'والجواب'. Understand how the author defends against the intermediate claim.
The objection uses logical terms like 'استغراق' (universality).
3
Grasp the per-action interpretation
Notice that the answer reinterprets 'all actions' to mean each action individually. This is key: it means every single action is either sound or unsound, not the organ's overall state.
This resolves the paradox of an organ being both healthy and ill.
4
Connect to the no-intermediate principle
Understand that if each action is either sound or unsound, then there is no action that is neither. Thus health and illness are strictly contradictory, with no middle ground.
This matches the principle of 'لا واسطة'.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check if you grasped the key logical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا واسطة بينهما أي بين الصحة والمرض المعرفين بهذين التعريفين إذ لا خروج عن النفي والإثبات. فالكيفية التي بها تصدر الأفعال عن موضوعها إما أن تكون أفعالها سليمة أو غير سليمة، فالأولى: هي الصحة، والثانية: المرض. قيل: عدمه إنما يظهر إذا عرف بحالة أو ملكة لا يصدر بها جميع الأفعال سليمة لا بما عرف به المصنف. فإنه إن أريد بلفظ الأفعال في التعريفين الاستغراق يلزم الواسطة وإن أريد به الجنس يلزم كون عضو واحد صحيحاً ومريضاً إذا كان بعض أفعاله سليما وبعضه غير سليم. والجواب: إن المراد الاستغراق لكن ليس المراد بقوله: يصدر عنهما الأفعال سليمة، أو غير سليمة أن يصدر عنه جميع الأفعال موصوفة بالسلامة أو بعدمها، وإلا لزم أن لا يتصف عضو بالصحة والمرض إلا بعد صدور كل فعل عنه، بل المراد أن كل فعل يصدر عنده يكون سليماً، أو لا يكون كل ما يصدر عنه سلباً بطريق رفع الإيجاب الكلي الشامل للسلب الكلي، والسلب عن البعض دون البعض، فلا واسطة.
There is no intermediate between them, i.e., between health and illness as defined by these two definitions, since there is no escape from affirmation and negation. For the quality by which actions issue from a subject either its actions are sound or unsound. The first is health, the second is illness. It was said: Its absence [of intermediate] only appears if it is defined as a state or disposition by which not all actions issue sound, not as defined by the author. For if by the word 'actions' in the two definitions unlimited universality is intended, then an intermediate would follow; and if the genus is intended, then one and the same member would be both healthy and ill if some of its actions are sound and some unsound. The answer: Unlimited universality is intended, but not that all actions issue sound or unsound from it, otherwise a member would not be described as healthy or ill until every action has issued from it. Rather, the meaning is that every action issuing from it is sound, or not all that issues is sound—by way of negation of the universal affirmative that includes the universal negative and negation from some without others. So there is no intermediate.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage argues that there is no intermediate state between health and illness because actions from a subject are either sound or unsound. An objection points out a difficulty: if 'actions' is taken universally (all actions), then an intermediate exists when some actions are sound and some not; if taken as a genus, then an organ could be both healthy and ill. The answer clarifies: the universal is intended, but it applies per action, not collectively. It means that each individual action is either sound or unsound. So for any given organ, each action is either sound or unsound; there is no action that is neither. Hence no intermediate state exists. This resolves the apparent paradox.

Key Terms
واسطة
استغراق
سلب
إيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an organ performs some sound actions and some unsound actions, it is in an intermediate state between health and illness.
Correction: The passage shows that 'all actions' means each action individually, so the organ is not in an intermediate state; rather, each action is either healthy or ill, and the organ is described as healthy or ill based on the per-action evaluation, not a global average.

Checkpoint
How does the passage resolve the objection that an organ could be both healthy and ill if its actions are mixed?
A) By denying that actions can be mixed.
B) By stating that universality applies to each action individually.
C) By redefining health and illness.
D) By accepting that there is an intermediate.

Key Takeaway
Health and illness are contradictory because each action is either sound or unsound, leaving no room for an intermediate category.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area in your life where you try to claim a middle ground between obedience and disobedience, hoping that some good actions will outweigh the bad? What would change if you examined each action separately as either fully for Allah or not?
?
The objector mistakenly thought that if an organ has both sound and unsound actions, it must be in an intermediate state. Do you ever judge your own spiritual state by looking at your overall average rather than the specifics of each intention and action? Which specific action today do you need to be honest about—is it sound or unsound?
?
This lesson strips away any comfortable 'in-between' when it comes to the health of the soul. Are you willing to abandon the middle ground and call each action what it is? What would that mean for a relationship, a habit, or a thought you have been tolerating as 'not that bad'?

Qualities Specific to Quantity: Two Ways They Attach to Quantity
Classification of quantity-specific qualities into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how qualities specific to quantity are classified into those that attach to quantity alone and those that attach together with another quality, with examples.

Core Idea
Qualities specific to quantity come either alone or with a companion.

In this lesson, we explore the first aim of the chapter on qualities specific to quantity. Al-Jurjani presents a binary classification: some qualities, like evenness and triangulation, attach to quantity by itself; others, like the composite form (khilqa), involve quantity together with another quality such as color. This sets the stage for a deeper discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Al-Jurjani divides the category.
The passage includes both the main text and commentary.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the two types: 'alone' (وحدها) and 'together with others' (مع غيرها). Note the examples for discrete and continuous quantity.
Examples for discrete: evenness, oddness, primality. For continuous: triangulation, quadrature.
3
Understand the commentary
The commentary clarifies what 'alone' and 'together with others' mean, especially for the composite 'form' (khilqa).
The commentary explains that 'together with others' involves a whole-part relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ الفصل الثالث ] من فصول الكيف ( في الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات وفيه مقصدان ) المقصد الأول : الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات ] ( المقصد الأول (أنها أي الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات ( عارضة للكم إما وحدها فللمنفصلة كالزوجية والفردية) العارضتين للعدد وكذلك الأولية والتركيب وسائر الأعراض الذاتية للأعداد ( وللمتصلة التثليث والتربيع) أي كالتثليث والتربيع فإنهما عارضان للمثلث ،والمربع وكذلك التخميس والتسديس وغيرهما من الهيئات العارضة للسطوح الكثيرة الأضلاع ) وإما مع غيرها كالخلقة، فإنها مجموع شكل وهو عارض للكم) المتصل من حيث أنه محاط بحد واحد أو أكثر (مع اعتبار لون قال الإمام قوله : ( يكون لفظياً ) أي راجعاً إلى تفسير لفظي الصحة والمرض. قوله : ( عارضة للكم) أي بالذات . قوله: (إما وحدها أي منفردة من غير انضمام أمر معه فيكون عائداً إلى ما ذكره الإمام بقوله : إما لنفسه . قوله: ( وإما مع غيرها ) أي عارضة لكم مقارنة مع غيره مقارنة الكل مع الجزء ليصح كون الخلقة مثالاً له ويصح كونها قسيماً لقوله : وحدها فإنه لو أجري على ظاهره، ورد عليه أنه كما هو عارض للكم وحدها عارض له مع كل ما يقارنه ومآله ما قال الإمام : إما لجزئه . قوله: (مع اعتبار لون ) أي لون معتبر معه. قوله : (مع اعتبار لون أي مع لون معتبر أورد على هذا أنَّ الشكل وإن كان الكيفيات من المختصة بالكميات إلا أن اللون من الكيفيات المحسوسة فكيف تكون الخلقة المركبة منهما من قبيل الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات والمركب من نوع ومما ليس ذلك النوع لا يندرج في أحدهما؟ وأجيب بأن هذا مبني على أن اللون من خواص السطح، وعلى هذا يكون اللون أيضاً من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات، ولا تنافي بين كون الكيفية محسوسة وكونها مختصة بالكم، وأما إنَّ الكلام في الكيفية المفردة فكيف يعد الخلقة المركبة من الكيفيتين مما نحن فيه فسيجيء جوابه، وأنت خبير بأن هذا التوجيه لا يلائم قول المصنف : وإما مع غيرها : كالخلقة فإنه لم يعتبر في الخلقة على هذا التوجيه غير الكيفية المختصة بالكميات لأن كلا جزأيها حينئذ منها فالأنسب لكلام المصنف أن يقال: اللون، وإن لم يكن من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات لنفوذه في الأعماق، إلا أنه يصدق على المركب منه ، ومن الشكل أنه كيفية مختصة بها، كما لا
[The third chapter] of the chapters of quality (al-kayf) (concerning qualities specific to quantities, and it contains two aims). The first aim: qualities specific to quantities. (The first aim: they, i.e., the qualities specific to quantities, are accidents of quantity. Either alone: for discrete [quantity] like evenness and oddness) which are accidents of number, as well as primality, compositeness, and the rest of the essential accidents of numbers. (And for continuous [quantity]: triangulation and quadrature), i.e., such as triangulation and quadrature, for they are accidents of the triangle and the square, as well as pentagonalization, hexagonalization, and other configurations that are accidents of many-sided surfaces. (Or together with others, like form (al-khilqa), for it is a composite of shape, which is an accident of continuous quantity inasmuch as it is surrounded by one or more boundaries, with consideration of color). Al-Imam said: His saying: (it becomes verbal) refers to the verbal interpretation of health and disease. His saying: (accidents of quantity) i.e., essentially. His saying: (either alone) i.e., separate without any other thing accompanying it, so it refers to what al-Imam mentioned by his saying: either for itself. His saying: (or together with others) i.e., accidental to quantity accompanied by something else, as a whole with a part, so that form (al-khilqa) can be an example of it and can be a counterpart to his saying 'alone'. For if it were taken literally, it would be objected that just as it is accidental to quantity alone, it is accidental to it with everything that accompanies it, and its purport is what al-Imam said: either for its part. His saying: (with consideration of color) i.e., a color considered with it. His saying: (with consideration of color) i.e., with a color considered. An objection was raised against this: that shape, although it is among the qualities specific to quantities, yet color is among the sensible qualities, so how can the composite of the two be of the category of qualities specific to quantities? And a composite of a type and what is not that type does not fall under either. And it was answered: that this is based on the fact that color is a property of the surface, and on this basis color too is among the qualities specific to quantities, and there is no contradiction between a quality being sensible and being specific to quantity. As for the discussion about the simple quality, how then is the composite of the two qualities counted among what we are discussing? Its answer will come later. And you are aware that this interpretation does not accord with the author's statement: 'or together with others, like form', for he did not consider in form on this interpretation anything other than the quality specific to quantity, because both of its parts are then from it. So the more appropriate for the author's statement is to say: color, even if it is not among the qualities specific to quantities due to its penetration into depths, yet it is true of the composite of it and shape that it is a quality specific to it, just as not...
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a classification of qualities specific to quantities (al-kayfiyyat al-mukhtassa bi al-kammiyyat). It begins by stating that these qualities are accidents of quantity, and they are divided into two types: those that accompany quantity alone, and those that accompany quantity together with something else. Examples of the first type for discrete quantity are evenness and oddness (zawjiyya wa fardiyya), primality (awwaliyya), compositeness (tarkib), and other essential accidents of number. For continuous quantity, examples are triangulation (tathlith) and quadrature (tarbi'), as well as pentagonalization, hexagonalization, and other configurations of many-sided surfaces. The second type, where quantity is accompanied by something else, is exemplified by form (khilqa), which is a composite of shape (shakl) and color (lawn). The shape is an accident of continuous quantity, and color is considered alongside it. The passage then presents an objection: shape is a quality specific to quantity, but color is a sensible quality; how can a composite of the two be classified under qualities specific to quantity? The answer given is that color is a property of the surface (sath), and the surface is a quantity, so color too is among the qualities specific to quantities. There is no contradiction between a quality being sensible and being specific to quantity. The commentator also notes that this interpretation may not perfectly align with the author's wording, suggesting that the author may have intended that the composite of shape and color is called a quality specific to quantity because the whole is described as such, even though one part (color) may not be inherently specific to quantity. The passage guards against misunderstanding the scope of this category and the possibility of composite qualities.

Key Terms
الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات
الزوجية والفردية
التثليث والتربيع
الخلقة
اللون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a quality specific to quantity that occurs 'alone' for continuous quantity?
A) Evenness
B) Primality
C) Triangulation
D) Form (khilqa)

Key Takeaway
Qualities specific to quantity are either attached to quantity alone or together with another quality, as seen in the composite 'form'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you tend to isolate qualities (like counting blessings) or see them as composites (like a blessing combined with its purpose)? How does recognizing both the 'alone' and 'together' aspects of Allah's creation affect your sense of awe and gratitude? This week, pick one blessing and deliberately reflect on both its individual quality and the composite of qualities that make it complete.
?
The scholars classified even the attributes of quantity with precision. How carefully do you examine the qualities of your own actions—like your prayer—both in isolation (its form) and together with other qualities (its intention and presence of heart)? What would change if you applied the same meticulousness to your worship starting today?
?
Consider the concept of 'form' as a composite of shape and color. In your life, are you focusing on the 'shape' (external actions) without the 'color' (inner sincerity)? Or vice versa? How can you ensure both are present in your deeds to present a complete and beautiful act of worship to Allah?

Resolving the Objection: Composite Shape-Color as Quantity-Specific
Objection and answer about composite form as quantity-specific quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised against considering the composite form (khilqa) as a quality specific to quantity and the answer provided by Al-Jurjani.

Core Idea
Even though color is sensible, it can still be quantity-specific as a property of surface.

Having classified qualities specific to quantity, Al-Jurjani anticipates an objection: how can a composite of shape (quantity-specific) and color (sensible) be considered a quality specific to quantity? In this lesson, we examine the objection and its resolution, which hinges on the nature of color as a property of the surface, itself a quantity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: it questions how a composite of two different types (quantity-specific and sensible) can fall under one category.
The objector expects that such a composite cannot be classed as quantity-specific.
2
Understand the answer
The answer is that color is a property of the surface, and the surface is a quantity, so color is also quantity-specific. There is no contradiction between being sensible and being quantity-specific.
This answer relies on the premise that color is a surface property.
3
Note the commentary's nuance
The commentator notes that this interpretation may not perfectly match the author's wording, suggesting that the author might have considered the composite as a whole to be quantity-specific even if color alone is not.
This shows the depth of scholarly analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Qualities Specific to Quantities: Shape and Constitution
Defines qualities specific to quantities with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how qualities specific to quantities are defined and how shape and constitution exemplify this category, including why constitution is considered accidental to quantity rather than the natural body.

Core Idea
Qualities specific to quantities attach to quantity itself, not directly to the body.

In this passage, al-Razi clarifies a subtle but crucial category in the classification of qualities: those that are inherently tied to quantities (like shape) or to parts of quantities (like constitution, via shape). He addresses a potential objection about constitution being tied to the natural body, and uses the example of color to reinforce the point that the primary carrier is always a magnitude. Understanding this helps us see how the kalam tradition analyzes attributes step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Razi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Distinguish the two types
Identify the two kinds of qualities specific to quantities: those that are accidental to quantity itself (like shape) and those accidental via a part (like constitution). Understand the difference.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
Read the objection that constitution seems accidental to the natural body. Then study al-Razi's response: the accident to quantity can be either as quantity or as quantity of a specific thing. Both are accidental to quantity.
This answer shows how kalam handles potential counterexamples.
4
Connect the color example
See how al-Razi uses the example of color to illustrate that the primary carrier (surface) is a magnitude, reinforcing that constitution's primary carrier is also magnitude.
This example solidifies the conclusion that constitution is essentially accidental to quantity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرازي هذا النوع من الكيفيات هو الكيفية التي تعرض أولا، وبالذات للكميات وبتوسطها لغيرها ويدخل في ذلك ما يكون كذلك إما لنفسه كالشكل العارض للمقدار، وإما لجزئه كالخلقة فإنها كذلك بواسطة جزئها الذي هو الشكل، فإن قيل: الخلقة عارضة للجسم الطبيعي إذ لولاه لم يكن خلقة قلنا العارض للكمية إما أن يعرض لها من حيث أنها كمية أو من حيث أنها كمية شيء مخصوص، وكلا القسمين عارض للكمية ثم إن اللون حامله الأول هو السطح الذي هو نهاية الجسم الطبيعي بتوسط الجسم التعليمي، ومعنى كون الجسم ملوناً أن سطحه ملون فكلا جزئي الخلقة حامله الأول هو المقدار فالخلقة عارضة بالذات للكم
Al-Razi: This type of qualities is the quality that occurs first and essentially to quantities and through them to others. Included in that is what is so either for itself, like shape (al-shakl) occurring to magnitude (al-miqdar), or for its part, like constitution (al-khilqa), for it is so by means of its part which is shape. So if it is said: Constitution is accidental to the natural body, for without it there would be no constitution, we say: The accidental to quantity either occurs to it insofar as it is quantity, or insofar as it is the quantity of a particular thing, and both divisions are accidental to quantity. Then indeed color (al-lawn): its primary carrier (ḥāmiluhu al-awwal) is the surface (al-saṭḥ) which is the limit of the natural body by means of the teaching body (al-jism al-taʿlīmī), and the meaning of a body being colored is that its surface is colored. Thus both parts of constitution have their primary carrier as magnitude, and so constitution is essentially accidental to quantity.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines the category of 'qualities specific to quantities' (al-kayfiyyāt al-mukhtaṣṣa bi al-kammiyyāt). The commentator explains that these are qualities that pertain firstly and essentially to quantities and through them to other things. He provides two kinds: (1) those that belong to quantity itself, like shape (shakl) which is an accident of magnitude; and (2) those that belong to a part of quantity, like constitution (khilqa), which is the form or figure of a body and is accidental to quantity by means of its part, namely shape. An objection is raised: constitution is accidental to the natural body, not to quantity, because if there were no natural body there would be no constitution. The commentator replies that whatever is accidental to quantity can be either accidental to it purely as quantity or as the quantity of a specific thing; both are accidental to quantity. He then uses the example of color to show that the primary carrier of each part of constitution is magnitude: color's primary carrier is surface (which is a magnitude), so constitution too is essentially accidental to quantity. The conclusion is that constitution is essentially accidental to quantity, not to the natural body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Constitution is accidental to the natural body directly.
Correction: Constitution is accidental to quantity via its part (shape), so its primary carrier is magnitude, not the natural body.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary carrier (al-ḥāmil al-awwal) of color?
A) The natural body (al-jism al-ṭabīʿī)
B) The teaching body (al-jism al-taʿlīmī)
C) The surface (al-saṭḥ)
D) Quantity (al-kammiyya)

Key Takeaway
Qualities specific to quantities attach to quantity itself or its part, not directly to the body, as shown by shape and constitution.

Are Color and Light Qualities of Quantity?
Objection and response on color/light classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether color and light should be classified as qualities specific to quantities, and how the commentator resolves it through the phrase 'with other than it'.

Core Idea
Color and light challenge neat classifications because they can penetrate bodies, not just surfaces.

This passage presents an objection to a previous definition: if color and light primarily inhere in surfaces, shouldn't they be part of qualities specific to quantities? A quick objection shows they can also penetrate bodies, complicating matters. The commentator clarifies the intended meaning to preserve the original classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: objection, counter-argument, and commentator's clarification.
Pay attention to the phrase 'قد يقال' which introduces the counter.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
The objection states that color and light should be included because their primary carrier (al-ḥāmil al-awwal) is the surface, and there is no color or light in the depth of a body.
3
Understand the counter-argument
The counter-argument notes that color can be penetrating (nāfidh) inside the body, and light from intrinsically luminous objects like the sun penetrates. So they are not confined to the surface.
This challenges the initial assumption.
4
Grasp the commentator's resolution
The commentator explains that the original phrase 'maʿa ghayrihā' (with other than it) means absolutely other than qualities specific to quantity, implying that even if color and light have some surface association, they involve other aspects and thus are not purely in that category.
This maintains the original classification while acknowledging the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ويتوجه على هذا أن يكون اللون والضوء داخلين في هذا النوع من الكيفيات، لأنَّ حاملهما الأول هو السطح إذ لا لون ولا ضوء في عمق الجسم، وقد يقال : اللون قد يكون نافذاً داخل الجسم وكذلك الضوء في المضيء بالذات كالشمس فلا يختصان بالسطح قوله : ( وقد يقال إلخ ) عديل لقوله : ثم إِنَّ اللون حامله الأول إلخ، فعلى هذا القول معنى قوله : مع غيرها مع غير الكيفية المختصة بالكم مطلقاً .
He said: And it follows from this that color and light are included in this type of qualities, because their primary carrier is the surface, since there is no color or light in the depth of the body. And it may be said: Color may be penetrating inside the body, and likewise light in that which is intrinsically luminous like the sun, so they are not specific to the surface. His statement: (And it may be said etc.) is a parallel to his statement: Then indeed color: its primary carrier etc. So according to this statement, the meaning of his statement: 'with other than it' (maʿa ghayrihā) is absolutely with other than the quality specific to quantity.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response regarding whether color and light belong to the category of qualities specific to quantities. The objection notes that since the primary carrier of color and light is the surface, they should be included. However, a counter-argument is raised: color and light can penetrate bodies (e.g., light from the sun penetrates the air, and color may be infused), so they are not confined to surfaces. The commentator clarifies that the phrase 'with other than it' (maʿa ghayrihā) in the original text is intended to mean absolutely other than the quality specific to quantity, implying that color and light are not purely in this category because they involve other factors. Thus, the passage highlights the debate over whether color and light fit strictly within the definition.

Correction: The passage shows that color and light can penetrate bodies (e.g., light from the sun penetrates the air, and color may be infused), so they are not confined to surfaces and involve other aspects.

Checkpoint

A) Their primary carrier is not the surface
B) They can penetrate inside bodies

Key Takeaway
Color and light do not belong strictly to qualities specific to quantities because they can penetrate bodies, thereby involving more than just surface properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you judged a situation or a person based only on what is 'on the surface,' like assuming color/light only belong to surfaces? How can this lesson's insight about penetration change how you approach such judgments in your own life? This week, identify one area where you have been superficial and commit to looking deeper.
?
The passage shows that even careful categorizations can have exceptions. Is there a belief or assumption you hold about your faith that you have never examined deeply? What would it cost you to honestly consider a deeper perspective, even if it challenges your current comfort?
?
If color and light can penetrate bodies, what does that teach you about how Allah's signs (like light in the Quran) might penetrate your heart? Are you content with merely knowing the surface of His signs, or do you let them penetrate you? What will you do today to allow one verse to go deeper than your mind?

Definition of the Planar Angle as a Pure Quality
Defines planar angle, clarifies it is a form not a composite, and contrasts with shape.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that an angle is defined as a form (hay'a) of enclosure of two lines, that it is not a composite of lines and surface, and how it differs from shape.

Core Idea
The angle is a simple quality of enclosure, not a composite figure.

The author begins by correcting a potential misunderstanding: the angle is not a composite thing like constitution (khilqa), but a simple quality. He then gives a precise definition of the planar angle as the concave form arising from the meeting of two lines on a surface. The passage also distinguishes the angle from shape (shakl), which requires complete enclosure.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic and translation of the definition: 'هيئة إحاطة الضلعين من بالسطح مثلا في ملتقاهما بالاستقامة'. Note that the angle is the form, not the composite.
2
Understand the explanation
The summary explains how the form arises: two lines meeting on a surface create a concave form. Notice that this does not require complete enclosure.
The term 'planar angle' is used specifically.
3
Distinguish from shape
Angle differs from shape: shape requires complete enclosure (like triangle needs three sides), while angle depends only on two lines.
This is important for the later classification debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمتبادر قوله : ( وكالزاوية) أن الزاوية كالخلقة في أنها مركبة من الكيفية المختصة بالكميات مع غيرها، وليست كذلك كما يدل عليه قوله : ( فإنها هيئة إحاطة الضلعين من بالسطح مثلا في ملتقاهما بالاستقامة ) فالزاوية هي تلك الهيئة لا الأمر المركب من تلك الهيئة والضلعين والسطح كما يتوهم، وأشار بقوله : مثلاً إلى أن ما ذكره تعريف للزاوية المسطحة دون مطلقها المتناول للزاوية المجسمة، وتلخيصه أنَّ الزاوية المسطحة هيئة عارضة للسطح عند ملتقى خطين يحيطان به من غير أن يتحدا خطاً واحداً، فإنه إذا اتصل خطان على نقطة في سطح من غير أن يتحدا كذلك عرض لذلك السطح عند ملتقاهما هيئة انحدابية فيما بين الخطين المتصلين هي الزاوية، وقد تطلق الزاوية على المقدار ذي الزاوية، كما يطلق الشكل على المشكل، وليس يعتبر في تحققها إحاطتهما بذلك السطح إحاطة تامة بل ربما امتنع إحاطتهما به كذلك كما إذا كان الخطان مستقيمين ولا يعتبر أيضاً أن يكون هناك خط آخر يحيط معهما ،به ولا أن يكون ذانك الخطان متناهيين أو غير متناهين قصيرين أو طويلين بخلاف الشكل إذ لا بد فيه من الإحاطة التامة ، فالشكل العارض للمثلث يتوقف على أضلاعه الثلاثة وكل واحدة من زواياه تتوقف على ضلعين فقط، فقولنا : من غير أن يتحدا احتراز عما إذا اتصل قوسان على نقطة وصارتا قوساً واحدة، وأما قوله : لا باستقامة فمستغنى عنه إذ لا إحاطة أصلاً مع الاستقامة، ثم إنَّ الزاوية على التعريف المذكور من مقولة الكيف ( ومنهم من جعل الزاوية من باب الكم) لقبولها التفاوت والتساوي ) وإنها ) أي ولأنها ) توصف بالأصغر والأكبر، وبكونها نصفاً وثلثاً) لزاوية أخرى ولا شك أنَّ هذه الصفات أعراض ذاتية للكم فتكون الزاوية كماً ولذلك عرف قوله : ( والمتبادر إلخ) الظاهر أنه عطف على قوله : كالخلقة فيكون مثالاً للحركة، وليس كذلك بل هي من قبيل الكيفية المختصة بالكم المتصل وحدها، إذ لا فرق بينها وبين الشكل في كون كل منهما ماهية إحاطة المقدار بالمقدار فقوله : كالزاوية عطف على قوله : للمثلث والمربع إلا أنه أخره عن موضعه لاحتياجه إلى التفصيل. قوله : ( في ملتقاهما) أي حاصلة في ملتقاهما قوله : ( لا) بالاستقامة) متعلق بملتقاهما . قوله : ( من غير أن يتحدا إلخ) بأن يكون الحد المشترك باقياً بحاله . قوله : أي) لأن هذا المعروض أي تذكر الضمير الراجح إلى الزاوية بتأويلها بالمعروض. اتصلا على نقطة وصارتا قوساً ،واحدة ، اللهم إلا أن يقال : لفظ الضلعين يخرجه إذ لا يطلق الضلع على شيء من تينك القوسين فليتأمل . قوله: (وتلخيصه أن الزاوية المسطحة إلخ) وعلى هذا فالزاوية المجسمة هيئة عارضة للجسم عند ملتقى سطحين يحيطان به من غير أن يتحدا.
And what is understood from his statement: (and like the angle) is that the angle (al-zāwiya) is like constitution in that it is composed of the quality specific to quantities together with other than it, but it is not so, as is indicated by his statement: (for it is the form of enclosure of the two sides upon the surface, for example, at their meeting without straightness). So the angle is that form, not the composite thing composed of that form and the two sides and the surface, as might be imagined. And by his statement: 'for example', he indicates that what he mentioned is a definition of the planar angle (al-zāwiya al-musaṭṭaḥa) only, not its absolute term encompassing the solid angle (al-zāwiya al-mujassama). The summary: the planar angle is a form that occurs to the surface at the meeting of two lines that enclose it without becoming one line. For if two lines meet at a point on a surface without so becoming one, there occurs to that surface at their meeting a concave (inḥidābiyya) form between the two connected lines, which is the angle. And the term angle may be applied to the magnitude having an angle, just as shape (shakl) is applied to the shaped thing. Its realization does not require complete enclosure of the surface by the two lines; indeed, enclosure may be impossible, as when the two lines are straight. Nor is it required that there be another line enclosing with them, nor that the two lines be finite or infinite, short or long—unlike shape, for which complete enclosure is necessary. Thus shape occurring to a triangle depends on its three sides, while each of its angles depends on only two sides. So our statement: 'without them becoming one' is a safeguard against the case where two arcs meet at a point and become one arc. As for his statement: 'without straightness' (lā bi-istiqāma), it is unnecessary, since there is no enclosure at all with straightness. Then the angle according to the mentioned definition is from the category of quality (al-kayf). Yet some make the angle from the category of quantity (al-kamm), because it admits variation and equality, and because it is described as smaller and larger, and as being half or a third of another angle. There is no doubt that these attributes are essential accidents of quantity, so the angle would be a quantity. Therefore he defined... His statement: (and what is understood etc.) apparently is coordinated with his statement: 'like constitution' and would then be an example of motion, but it is not so; rather it belongs to the quality specific to continuous quantity alone, since there is no difference between it and shape in that each is the essence of enclosure of magnitude by magnitude. So his statement: 'like the angle' is coordinated with his statement: 'like triangle and square', but he postponed it because it needs detail. His statement: (at their meeting) means occurring at their meeting. His statement: (without straightness) is attached to 'their meeting'. His statement: (without them becoming one etc.) means that the common boundary remains as it is. His statement: (because this subject) means remember the pronoun referring to the angle by interpreting it as the subject. They meet at a point and become one arc, unless it is said: the word 'two sides' (al-ḍilʿayn) excludes it, for 'side' is not applied to either of those two arcs—so consider. His statement: (and its summary that the planar angle etc.) and on this basis, the solid angle is a form occurring to the body at the meeting of two surfaces that enclose it without them becoming one.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines and analyzes the concept of an angle (zāwiya) within the discussion of qualities specific to quantities. The commentator first corrects a misunderstanding: unlike constitution (khilqa), the angle is not composite of a quality specific to quantity and something else—rather it is a simple quality. The definition given is 'the form of enclosure of the two sides upon the surface at their meeting without straightness' (hayʾat iḥāṭat al-ḍilʿayn bi al-saṭḥ ʿinda multaqāhumā bi-ghayr istiqāma). The angle itself is that form, not a composite of form, sides, and surface. The definition is specifically for planar angles (zāwiya musaṭṭaḥa), not solid angles. The summary explains: when two lines meet on a surface without merging into one line, a concave form (hayʾa inḥidābiyya) appears at their meeting—that is the planar angle. The term angle can also be used metonymically for the magnitude that has the angle. Unlike shape (shakl), the angle does not require complete enclosure; for example, straight lines do not enclose a finite area, yet they form an angle. Shape needs complete closure (like a triangle requires three sides), whereas an angle depends only on two lines. The safeguard 'without them becoming one' excludes arcs that merge into a single arc. The statement 'without straightness' is redundant because straight lines cannot enclose a surface. Then the passage addresses the classification of angle: according to the definition, angle belongs to the category of quality (kayf), but some scholars argue it belongs to quantity (kamm) because angles can be compared as equal, larger, smaller, half, third—these are properties of quantity. The commentator notes that if angle were quantity, those attributes would be essential accidents, but the definition as quality seems more precise. Finally, he explains textual connections: the appearance of 'like angle' in the original text is coordinated with 'like triangle and square', not with 'like constitution', and it is delayed because it requires elaboration. The concluding note defines the solid angle as a form occurring to a body at the meeting of two enclosing surfaces without merging.

هيئة إحاطة
الضلعين
السطح
الزاوية المسطحة
الشكل
المقدار

Correction: The passage clarifies that the angle is only the form of enclosure, not the composite.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the planar angle?
A) The composite of two lines and the surface
B) The form of enclosure of two lines meeting on a surface
C) The magnitude of the space between two lines
D) The shape formed by three lines

Key Takeaway
An angle is not a figure but a simple quality—the form of enclosure of two lines meeting on a surface.

Classification of Angle: Quality or Quantity?
Presents the classification debate (quality vs quantity) and defines solid angle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether an angle belongs to the category of quality or quantity, and the evidence for each position.

Core Idea
Some classify angle as quantity because it admits comparison; but the definition suggests it is a quality.

After defining the angle, the passage addresses a classification dispute: is an angle a quality (kayf) or a quantity (kamm)? The author notes the default view is quality, but some argue for quantity because angles can be equal, larger, smaller, and fractions. The passage also defines the solid angle in this context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The passage states: 'from the category of quality' (al-kayf) as default, then 'some make it from quantity' (al-kamm). Find these statements.
2
Examine the evidence for quantity
The reason given: angles admit variation and equality, and are described as larger, smaller, half, third. These are essential accidents of quantity.
3
Understand the author's textual clarification
The author explains the textual coordination of 'like the angle' to clarify the intended meaning.
4
Learn the solid angle definition
The passage ends with a definition of the solid angle as a form from two surfaces meeting without merging.
This ties back to the planar definition.

لا فرق بينها وبين الشكل في كون كل منهما ماهية إحاطة المقدار بالمقدار فقوله : كالزاوية عطف على قوله : للمثلث والمربع إلا أنه أخره عن موضعه لاحتياجه إلى التفصيل. قوله : ( في ملتقاهما) أي حاصلة في ملتقاهما قوله : ( لا) بالاستقامة) متعلق بملتقاهما . قوله : ( من غير أن يتحدا إلخ) بأن يكون الحد المشترك باقياً بحاله . قوله : أي) لأن هذا المعروض أي تذكر الضمير الراجح إلى الزاوية بتأويلها بالمعروض. اتصلا على نقطة وصارتا قوساً ،واحدة ، اللهم إلا أن يقال : لفظ الضلعين يخرجه إذ لا يطلق الضلع على شيء من تينك القوسين فليتأمل . قوله: (وتلخيصه أن الزاوية المسطحة إلخ) وعلى هذا فالزاوية المجسمة هيئة عارضة للجسم عند ملتقى سطحين يحيطان به من غير أن يتحدا.
And what is understood from his statement: (and like the angle) is that the angle (al-zāwiya) is like constitution in that it is composed of the quality specific to quantities together with other than it, but it is not so, as is indicated by his statement: (for it is the form of enclosure of the two sides upon the surface, for example, at their meeting without straightness). So the angle is that form, not the composite thing composed of that form and the two sides and the surface, as might be imagined. And by his statement: 'for example', he indicates that what he mentioned is a definition of the planar angle (al-zāwiya al-musaṭṭaḥa) only, not its absolute term encompassing the solid angle (al-zāwiya al-mujassama). The summary: the planar angle is a form that occurs to the surface at the meeting of two lines that enclose it without becoming one line. For if two lines meet at a point on a surface without so becoming one, there occurs to that surface at their meeting a concave (inḥidābiyya) form between the two connected lines, which is the angle. And the term angle may be applied to the magnitude having an angle, just as shape (shakl) is applied to the shaped thing. Its realization does not require complete enclosure of the surface by the two lines; indeed, enclosure may be impossible, as when the two lines are straight. Nor is it required that there be another line enclosing with them, nor that the two lines be finite or infinite, short or long—unlike shape, for which complete enclosure is necessary. Thus shape occurring to a triangle depends on its three sides, while each of its angles depends on only two sides. So our statement: 'without them becoming one' is a safeguard against the case where two arcs meet at a point and become one arc. As for his statement: 'without straightness' (lā bi-istiqāma), it is unnecessary, since there is no enclosure at all with straightness. Then the angle according to the mentioned definition is from the category of quality (al-kayf). Yet some make the angle from the category of quantity (al-kamm), because it admits variation and equality, and because it is described as smaller and larger, and as being half or a third of another angle. There is no doubt that these attributes are essential accidents of quantity, so the angle would be a quantity. Therefore he defined... His statement: (and what is understood etc.) apparently is coordinated with his statement: 'like constitution' and would then be an example of motion, but it is not so; rather it belongs to the quality specific to continuous quantity alone, since there is no difference between it and shape in that each is the essence of enclosure of magnitude by magnitude. So his statement: 'like the angle' is coordinated with his statement: 'like triangle and square', but he postponed it because it needs detail. His statement: (at their meeting) means occurring at their meeting. His statement: (without straightness) is attached to 'their meeting'. His statement: (without them becoming one etc.) means that the common boundary remains as it is. His statement: (because this subject) means remember the pronoun referring to the angle by interpreting it as the subject. They meet at a point and become one arc, unless it is said: the word 'two sides' (al-ḍilʿayn) excludes it, for 'side' is not applied to either of those two arcs—so consider. His statement: (and its summary that the planar angle etc.) and on this basis, the solid angle is a form occurring to the body at the meeting of two surfaces that enclose it without them becoming one.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines and analyzes the concept of an angle (zāwiya) within the discussion of qualities specific to quantities. The commentator first corrects a misunderstanding: unlike constitution (khilqa), the angle is not composite of a quality specific to quantity and something else—rather it is a simple quality. The definition given is 'the form of enclosure of the two sides upon the surface at their meeting without straightness' (hayʾat iḥāṭat al-ḍilʿayn bi al-saṭḥ ʿinda multaqāhumā bi-ghayr istiqāma). The angle itself is that form, not a composite of form, sides, and surface. The definition is specifically for planar angles (zāwiya musaṭṭaḥa), not solid angles. The summary explains: when two lines meet on a surface without merging into one line, a concave form (hayʾa inḥidābiyya) appears at their meeting—that is the planar angle. The term angle can also be used metonymically for the magnitude that has the angle. Unlike shape (shakl), the angle does not require complete enclosure; for example, straight lines do not enclose a finite area, yet they form an angle. Shape needs complete closure (like a triangle requires three sides), whereas an angle depends only on two lines. The safeguard 'without them becoming one' excludes arcs that merge into a single arc. The statement 'without straightness' is redundant because straight lines cannot enclose a surface. Then the passage addresses the classification of angle: according to the definition, angle belongs to the category of quality (kayf), but some scholars argue it belongs to quantity (kamm) because angles can be compared as equal, larger, smaller, half, third—these are properties of quantity. The commentator notes that if angle were quantity, those attributes would be essential accidents, but the definition as quality seems more precise. Finally, he explains textual connections: the appearance of 'like angle' in the original text is coordinated with 'like triangle and square', not with 'like constitution', and it is delayed because it requires elaboration. The concluding note defines the solid angle as a form occurring to a body at the meeting of two enclosing surfaces without merging.

The Definition of the Plane Angle and Its Classification
Defines the plane angle and discusses its quality/quantity categorization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a plane angle in Islamic logic and the debate over whether it belongs to the category of quality or quantity.

Core Idea
The angle is a state, not a composite, and its nature as quality or quantity is debated.

In this passage from a classical work of logic and metaphysics, the author defines the plane angle (al-zāwiya al-musaṭṭaḥa) and addresses a key classification issue: is it a quality or a quantity? Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how scholars analyzed geometric entities within the Aristotelian category framework. Let's explore the definition and the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Focus on the first part where the author says the angle is a state (hay'a) that arises when two lines meet without becoming one. Note that it is not a composite of line and surface.
The definition is specific to the plane angle, not the solid angle.
2
Identify the conditions
The author emphasizes that the two lines need not completely enclose a surface, unlike a shape (shakl). For example, a triangle's shape depends on three sides, but each angle on only two.
This clarifies that angle is a simpler notion than shape.
3
Examine the classification debate
The passage states that according to the definition, the angle is from the category of quality. But some argue it is quantity because it admits inequality and equality, and can be described as half or third.
These attributes are essential accidents of quantity.
4
Understand the supporting arguments
Those who consider it quantity define it as a surface surrounded by two lines meeting at a point. Reflect on why this categorization matters for ontology.

For example, at their meeting point with straightness, the angle is that state, not the composite thing composed of that state and the two sides and the surface as is imagined. And he indicated by his saying 'for example' that what he mentioned is a definition of the plane angle, not the absolute angle encompassing the solid angle. And its summary is that the plane angle is an accidental state of the surface at the meeting of two lines that surround it without the two lines becoming one line. For when two lines meet at a point on a surface without becoming one, there appears on that surface at their meeting point a curved state between the two connected lines, which is the angle. And sometimes the angle is called for the quantity possessing the angle, just as the shape is called for the thing having a shape. And it is not considered for its realization that the two lines completely surround that surface; rather, perhaps their surrounding it is impossible, as when the two lines are straight. Nor is it considered that there be another line that surrounds with them, nor that those two lines be finite or infinite, short or long, unlike the shape, for which complete surrounding is necessary. For the shape accidental to the triangle depends on its three sides, and each one of its angles depends on only two sides. So our saying 'without them becoming one' is a precautionary exclusion for when two arcs meet at a point and become one arc. And as for his saying 'not with straightness', it is dispensable, since there is no surrounding at all with straightness. Then the angle according to the mentioned definition is from the category of quality (and some make the angle from the category of quantity) because it admits inequality and equality, and because it is described as smaller and larger, and as being half and third of another angle, and there is no doubt that these attributes are essential accidents of quantity, so the angle is quantity, and therefore it was defined... He said: (and the apparent etc.) Apparently it is coordinate with his saying 'like the complexion' and would be an example of motion, but it is not so; rather, it is from the type of quality specific to continuous quantity alone, since there is no difference between it and shape in each being the essence of the surrounding of quantity by quantity. So his saying 'like the angle' is coordinate with his saying 'for the triangle and square', except that he delayed it from its place because it needed detail. He said: (at their meeting point) i.e., occurring at their meeting point. He said: (not with straightness) is attached to 'their meeting point'. He said: (without them becoming one etc.) meaning that the common boundary remains as it is. He said: (i.e., because this subject) i.e., the pronoun referring to the angle is interpreted as the subject. They met at a point and became one arc, unless it is said that the term 'two sides' excludes it, since the term 'side' is not applied to anything of those two arcs, so let it be considered. He said: (and its summary is that the plane angle etc.) and on this, the solid angle is an accidental state of the body at the meeting of two surfaces that surround it without them becoming one. The plane angle is that it is a surface surrounded by two lines that meet at a point without them becoming one line.

Commentary
This passage defines the plane angle (al-zāwiya al-musaṭṭaḥa) and addresses its categorization as a quality (kayf) or quantity (kamm). It begins by explaining the definition: the angle is a state (hay'a) that appears on a surface when two lines meet without becoming one. The author clarifies that this definition applies specifically to the plane angle, not the solid angle (zāwiya mujassama). He details conditions: the two lines need not completely surround the surface, unlike a shape (shakl) which requires complete enclosure. For example, a triangle's shape depends on three sides, but each of its angles depends on only two sides. The phrase 'without them becoming one' excludes arcs that merge into a single arc. He then notes that according to this definition, the angle is from the category of quality, but some scholars place it in the category of quantity because it admits inequality (tafāwut) and equality (tasāwī), and is described as smaller/larger, half/third. These are essential accidents of quantity, so they argue the angle is quantity and define it accordingly as a surface surrounded by two lines meeting at a point. The passage also includes commentary on specific phrases from the original text, explaining the relationship of terms and clarifying that the solid angle is a similar state arising from two surfaces.
