The Non-Essentiality Argument: Angle as Accident of Quantity
Refutes the essentiality claim using accidental occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the occurrence of inequality and divisibility to an angle does not prove it is a quantity, because the angle may be an accident of quantity.

Core Idea
Attributes of quantity can belong to angle accidentally through its substrate.

The first refutation targets the assumption that inequality and divisibility are essential to the angle. The author argues that these may occur accidentally because the angle itself is an accident of quantity, much like shape inherits properties from its substrate. This challenges the very basis of classifying angle as a quantity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Note the phrase 'عارض للكم'—'accident of quantity'—which is the key concept.
2
Identify the refutation's structure
Recognize that the passage responds to an earlier claim: that inequality/divisibility prove angle is quantity. The refutation says those attributes may be accidental.
The argument uses 'ممنوع' (not granted) to reject the claim of essentiality.
3
Understand the analogy with shape
Consider the example of shape, which is an accident of quantity and thus its properties are derived. Similarly, angle may borrow its properties from quantity without being a quantity itself.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does this matter for theology? The classification of entities into categories affects arguments about creation and attributes.
This connects to broader kalam discussions about accidents and substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب أنه أي هذا الاستدلال (إنما يتم أنَّ لو كان عروض ذلك ) التفاوت والتجزؤ ( لها ) أي للزاوية (بالذات) حتى يلزم كونها كماً ( وأنه ممنوع بل) عروضه لها يجوز أن يكون ) لأنه ( أي لأن هذا المعروض الذي هو الزاوية ( عارض للكم) كما في الشكل، فإنه يعرض له ذلك بواسطة معروضه الذي هو الكم ( ويبطله ) أي يبطل كون الزاوية من الكم ( أنها تبطل بالتضعيف وتنعدم أما القائمة فإنها كلها تبطل بالتضعيف مرة واحدة بحيث لا تبقى هناك زاوية أصلا وأما الحادة فإنها تبطل إذا كانت نصف قائمة بالتضعيف ،مرتين كذلك ( بخلاف الكم فإنه يزيد ) بالتضعيف ولا يبطل فلا تكون الزاوية القائمة ولا الحادة المذكورة من مقولة الكم، فلا يكون يتصل الضلعان على الاستقامة فلا ينفي الإحاطة فضلاً عن الزاوية . قوله : ( كذلك ) أي بحيث لا تبقى زاوية . قوله : ( ولو أبدل إلخ) لا يخفى أنَّ حاصل الاستدلال أنَّ الزاوية تبطل بالتضعيف ولا شيء من الكم كذلك، أما الكبرى فلأنَّ التضعيف زيادة في الكم لا إبطال له وأما الصغرى فلأن الحادة أي حادة كانت تنتهي بالتضعيف مرة أو مراراً إلى قائمة أو منفرجة وكل منهما يبطل بالتضعيف مرة، أما القائمة فللالتقاء الخطين على الاستقامة بحيث يصيران خطاً واحداً، وأما المنفرجة فلتأدية تضعيفها إلى الاستقامة مع زيادة لأنه لا بد في تضعيف المنفرجة من زيادة القدر الذي قوله : (بالتضعيف (مرتين أراد به أن يضعف مرة ثم يضعف الحاصل بالتضعيف الأول إلا أن يزاد على الحادة مثلها مرتين فإنَّ الحادة التي هي نصف قائمة إنما تبطل إذا ضعفت على هذا الوجه ثلاث مرات كما لا يخفي . قوله : إذا ضعفت مراراً كأنه أراد به ما فوق الواحد إذا الحادة التي هي أكبر من نصف القائمة إذا ضعفت مرتين يحصل ما ذكره كما لا يخفى على المتأمل.
And the answer: this inference is only valid if the occurrence of that (inequality and divisibility) to it (i.e., the angle) is essential, so that it would necessitate that it is a quantity. But that is not granted; rather, its occurrence to it may be because this subject—the angle—is an accident of quantity, as in the case of shape, for shape acquires that by means of its subject which is quantity. And what refutes it (i.e., refutes that the angle is from quantity) is that it is annihilated by doubling and ceases to exist. As for the right angle, it is completely annihilated by one doubling so that no angle remains. As for the acute angle, if it is half a right angle, it is annihilated by two doublings likewise. Contrary to quantity, which increases by doubling and is not annihilated. Therefore, the right angle and the mentioned acute angle are not from the category of quantity, so the absolute angle is also not from this category. And if one replaces doubling with increase, it would include the annihilation of all angles, for every angle increased until it equals two right angles, no angle remains. As for doubling, it may not annihilate the obtuse angle nor the acute that is smaller than half a right angle or larger, because there remains an angle on the other side of the other line. Yes, from doubling the obtuse angle some of it is annihilated, and likewise for that acute if doubled repeatedly. And this may suffice for the proof, because quantity when doubled nothing of it is annihilated, but rather it increases forever. And among the indications that the angle is not a surface is that it does not admit division parallel to the base, for the line connecting... He said: (such that no angle remains) because the two sides meet in a straight line, which does not negate surrounding, let alone the angle. He said: (likewise) i.e., such that no angle remains. He said: (and if one replaces etc.) It is not hidden that the summary of the proof is that the angle is annihilated by doubling and nothing of quantity is so. As for the major premise, because doubling is an increase in quantity, not an annihilation of it. As for the minor premise, because the acute angle, whatever acute it may be, ends by one doubling or repeated doublings to a right angle or an obtuse angle, and each of them is annihilated by the next doubling: the right angle because the two lines meet in a straight line so they become one line; the obtuse angle because its doubling leads to straightness with increase, for in doubling the obtuse angle an amount must be added. He said: (by doubling twice) he meant that one doubles once, then doubles the result of the first doubling, unless the acute is increased by its like twice, for the acute that is half a right angle is only annihilated if doubled in this manner three times, as is not hidden. He said: (if doubled repeatedly) he apparently meant more than one, for the acute that is greater than half a right angle, if doubled twice what he mentioned obtains, as is not hidden to the careful observer.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the view that the angle belongs to the category of quantity (kamm). It begins by responding to the earlier argument that the angle admits inequality and divisibility, which are essential accidents of quantity. The refutation argues that those attributes may be accidental to the angle because the angle itself is an accident of quantity (like shape), so the attributes derive from its substrate—thus the angle is not essentially quantity. The second and more decisive argument is based on doubling (tadʿīf). Unlike quantity, which increases when doubled, the angle is annihilated by doubling. For example, a right angle doubled becomes a straight line (no angle). An acute angle that is half a right angle requires two doublings to annihilate. The case of obtuse and certain acute angles is more nuanced: doubling an obtuse angle may not completely annihilate it because a smaller angle remains on the other side, but repeated doubling eventually eliminates it. The author then discusses a variation: if instead of doubling one simply increases the angle until it equals two right angles, all angles are annihilated. The concluding commentary clarifies the structure of the proof and provides examples of how different acute angles are annihilated at different doublings. The passage also includes a remark that angle is not a surface because it does not admit division parallel to the base (incomplete in OCR).

Key Terms
عارض
كم
بالذات
ممنوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inequality and divisibility in an angle prove it is a quantity.
Correction: These attributes may be accidental, derived from the angle's being an accident of quantity, not from its own essence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the inference is not valid?
A) Because angles never have inequality or divisibility.
B) Because inequality and divisibility might occur accidentally.
C) Because quantity itself is not a real category.
D) Because the angle is a substance.

Key Takeaway
The angle's properties may be borrowed from quantity, so angle is not necessarily a quantity itself.

The author denies the opponent's inference because it assumes essentiality without proof. The Doubling Argument: Why Angle Cannot Be Quantity
Proves angle is not quantity through annihilation by doubling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phenomenon of annihilation by doubling demonstrates that an angle cannot belong to the category of quantity.

Core Idea
Angle is annihilated by doubling; quantity is not.

The second and more direct refutation uses a simple test: doubling. Quantities increase when doubled, but angles vanish. The passage explores various cases—right, acute, obtuse—and shows that every angle eventually disappears, unlike any quantity. This conclusive proof establishes that angle cannot be quantity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the entire Arabic passage on the doubling argument along with the translation. Pay attention to the different angle types mentioned.
The passage includes commentary (قوله) that clarifies the reasoning.
2
Identify the main premise
The key premise: quantity increases when doubled, but angle is annihilated. Find the examples of right angle and acute half.
Right angle: one doubling → no angle. Acute half: two doublings → annihilation.
3
Examine the nuanced cases
Notice that obtuse and some acute angles require more doublings but still eventually vanish. The author also considers replacing doubling with increase to cover all angles.
The commentary explains why obtuse and acute angles less than half a right angle do not vanish immediately.
4
Understand the logical structure
The argument has a minor premise (angle is annihilated by doubling) and a major premise (no quantity is annihilated by doubling). Confirm both.
The minor is proven by examining all types; the major is self-evident.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Conclude: since angle behaves opposite to quantity, it cannot be from the category of quantity. This affects theological discussions about accidents and substances.
This argument is considered decisive by the author.

And the answer: this inference is only valid if the occurrence of that (inequality and divisibility) to it (i.e., the angle) is essential, so that it would necessitate that it is a quantity. But that is not granted; rather, its occurrence to it may be because this subject—the angle—is an accident of quantity, as in the case of shape, for shape acquires that by means of its subject which is quantity. And what refutes it (i.e., refutes that the angle is from quantity) is that it is annihilated by doubling and ceases to exist. As for the right angle, it is completely annihilated by one doubling so that no angle remains. As for the acute angle, if it is half a right angle, it is annihilated by two doublings likewise. Therefore, the right angle and the mentioned acute angle are not from the category of quantity, so the absolute angle is also not from this category. Why an Angle Is Not a Surface
Proof that angle is not a surface due to lack of parallel division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that an angle cannot be a surface because it lacks divisibility parallel to the base.

Core Idea
Angles cannot be surfaces because they resist division parallel to the base.

We now examine a clear geometric demonstration that an angle is fundamentally different from a surface. The argument hinges on a property surfaces possess that angles lack: divisibility parallel to the base. By understanding this proof, you will see why Muslim theologians and geometers distinguished between these two geometric entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it begins by stating what indicates the angle is not a surface, then gives the reason.
The proof relies on a property of surfaces: they can be divided by lines parallel to the base.
2
Understand the key property: division parallel to the base
Surfaces like triangles or squares can be divided into smaller similar shapes by drawing lines parallel to the base. The angle itself cannot be divided in this way.
Try drawing a triangle and drawing a line parallel to the base: you get a smaller triangle and a trapezoid, but the angle at the top remains.
3
Analyze the consequence
When a line connects the two sides of an angle, it creates a triangle. The original angle becomes one of the triangle's angles, not divided. Thus the angle does not behave like a surface.
This shows that the angle does not have the key property of surfaces, so it must be a different kind of entity.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
This proof illustrates how geometric distinctions are made based on properties like divisibility. It also shows the precision of kalam reasoning about physical entities.
This is part of a larger discussion about the nature of continuous quantities (angles, lines, surfaces, bodies).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على أنَّ الزاوية ليست سطحاً أنها لا تقبل الانقسام على موازاة الوتر فإنَّ الخط الواصل بين ضلعيها يحدث مثلثاً هي بعينها إحدى زواياه كما يشهد به التخيل الصحيح واتفاق المهندسين عليه قاطبة
And what indicates that the angle is not a surface is that it does not accept division parallel to the base; for the line connecting its two sides produces a triangle which is itself one of its angles, as sound imagination and the unanimous agreement of engineers witness.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This short passage gives an additional proof that an angle is not a surface. It argues that an angle does not admit division parallel to the base (the line joining the two sides). When such a line is drawn, it creates a triangle, but the angle remains as one of the triangle's angles, not divided. Thus, angle lacks the property of surfaces (divisibility in parallel), so it is not a surface.

Key Terms
زاوية (angle)
سطح (surface)
انقسام (division)
وتر (base/hypotenuse)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It cannot be measured in degrees.
B) It does not accept division parallel to the base.
C) It does not have any length.
D) It is not a continuous quantity.

Key Takeaway
An angle is not a surface because it cannot be divided by a line parallel to its base, a property that all surfaces possess.

Five Scholarly Opinions on the Nature of an Angle
Enumeration of five scholarly opinions on the nature of angle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five different opinions that classical scholars held about the ontological status of an angle, and the refutation of the opinion that it is a relation.

Core Idea
An angle is not simply contact; scholars debated whether it is a surface, quantity, relation, position, or privation.

Classical scholars debated whether an angle is a real entity or a mere mental construct. In this passage, the author lists five different opinions from various thinkers, and briefly critiques one of them. As you read, pay attention to how each opinion defines the angle's nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the five opinions
Scan the Arabic and translation to list the five views. Note that the first two (surface and quantity) are not fully described in this passage, but the last three are.
The author only elaborates on the relation, position, and privative views here.
2
Understand the critique of the relation view
Focus on why the author rejects the idea that angle is mere contact. The key is that contact lacks magnitude, but angles have size.
3
Compare the five views
Think about how each view defines the angle's nature. Which ones are more physical? Which are more metaphysical?
4
Consider why this matters for kalam
Angles appear in arguments about space, indivisible parts, and divine attributes. Understanding their nature has theological implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من جعل الزاوية من الإضافة، فقال : هي تماس خطين من غير أن يتحدا وبطلانه ،ظاهر فإن التماس لا يوصف بالصغر والكبر بخلاف الزاوية ومنهم من جعلها من مقولة الوضع وذهب جماعة إلى أنها أمر عدمي أعني انتهاء السطح عند نقطة مشتركة بين خطين يحيطان ،به فهذه أقوال خمسة أوردها بعضهم في رسالة صنفها لتحقيق الزاوية وما قيل فيها .
And among them is one who made the angle a relation (al-idafa), saying: it is the contact of two lines without being one. Its falsity is apparent, for contact is not described as small or large, unlike the angle. And among them is one who made it from the category of position (al-wad'). And a group held that it is a privative thing (amr 'adami), meaning the termination of the surface at a common point between two lines that enclose it. So these are five opinions which some have mentioned in a treatise he wrote for the investigation of the angle and what has been said about it.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage lists five opinions on the ontological status of an angle. The first (implied earlier) is that it is a surface. The second is that it is a quantity (previous lesson). The third is that it is a relation (al-idafa): contact of two lines, but this is refuted because contact is not quantifiable. The fourth is that it is a position (al-wad'). The fifth is that it is a privative entity (amr 'adami): the endpoint of a surface at a common point. The author does not decide, just reports the five opinions.

Key Terms
زاوية (angle)
إضافة (relation)
وضع (position)
عدمي (privative)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An angle is simply the contact of two lines.
Correction: Contact does not have magnitude, but angles are measured by size, so angle cannot be mere contact.

Checkpoint

A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The nature of an angle was debated among classical scholars, with opinions ranging from it being a real entity (surface, quantity) to a relation, position, or privation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you commit to studying the definitions of key Islamic concepts with the same precision?

The author refutes one opinion by pointing out a contradiction: contact cannot be described as small or large, but angles can. Take a moment to identify one attribute you believe you understand, and ask yourself: is there any hidden inconsistency in how you define it? Will you investigate further?

Do you feel uncomfortable with uncertainty, or can you hold the tension while seeking the truth? The Rule Against Compounds in the Ten Categories
Al-Sanusi states the rule that categories only contain simples and explains that considering compounds would produce infinite categories, making khalqah's classification problematic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the ten Aristotelian categories contain only simple types and why considering compounds leads to infinite categories, and how this rule makes the classification of khalqah problematic.

Core Idea
Categories are simple—compounds break the system.

In this note, al-Sanusi addresses a subtle classification issue: how can khalqah—a combination of shape and color—be considered a single quality when categories are only supposed to contain simple, non-composite types? Before examining the proposed solution, we must first understand the fundamental rule that each of the ten categories admits only simples. This lesson establishes that rule and shows why khalqah seems to violate it.

Guided Path
1
Read the rule about categories
Read the opening lines where al-Sanusi states that only simple types fall under one category. Notice the explicit condition: 'ما يندرج تحت واحدة منها فقط'.
This is the backbone of the entire discussion.
2
Understand the consequence of considering compounds
Al-Sanusi explains that if compounds were allowed, infinite categories would arise from pairings. Imagine the chaos of classification.
The phrase 'غير متناهية' emphasizes the problem.
3
Connect the rule to khalqah
Since khalqah is a composite of two different types (shape and color), it should not be counted among the simple qualities. Pause to see why this is a direct conclusion.
4
Reflect on the importance of this rule
Consider why a coherent categorical system must exclude compounds. This will help you appreciate the later justification.

تنبيه ) على ما يرد على جعل الخلقة من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات وهو أن المعتبر من أنواع المقولات العشر ما يندرج تحت واحدة منها فقط ( ولو اعتبر المركبات ) في المقولات وأنواعها حصلت مقولات غير متناهية ) أي غير محصورة بل كثيرة جدا بحسب الازدواجات الحاصلة بينهما ثناء وثلاث إلى عشار، وحصلت أيضاً أنواع غير منحصرة، فيما ذكروه من أنواعها بحسب التركيب الممكن فيما كان تركب مثلاً تلك الأنواع بين الأقسام الأربعة التي للكيف بعضها مع بعض، وعلى هذا كان ينبغي أن لا تعد الخلقة من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات لكونها مركبة من نوعين متخالفين (و) لكنهم قالوا : ( الخلقة إنما اعتبرت ) وجعلت داخلة في هذا النوع ( باعتبار وحدة ) عرضت لمجموع الشكل واللون (بحسبها ) أي بحسب تلك الوحدة ( يتصف بالحسن الشكل إذا قارن اللون وحصلت منهما كيفية وحدانية باعتبارها يصلح والقبح ) يعني أن يقال للشيء أنه حسن الصورة أو قبيح الصورة ( وهما ) أي الحسن والقبح بحسب الصورة (غير) الحسن والقبح ) العارضين للشكل وحده أو للون وحده ) قال المصنف : ( وهذا عذر غير واضح لأنهم إِنْ ادعوا أنَّ بين الشكل واللون وحدة حقيقة منعناها، وإن اكتفوا بالوحدة الاعتبارية جاز اعتبارها في كل أمرين يجتمعان، وقد يقال : قد اعتبر الوحدة بينهما في متعارف الناس حيث عبر عنهما بالخلقة ووصف الشخص بحسبهما بحسن الصورة وقبحها فلذلك عددناهما كيفية واحدة وأدرجناها فيما اندرج فيه جزؤها كما عرفت ولم نجد لها نظيراً في ذلك فاكتفينا بها .
A note on what is said regarding making khalqah (form/color) among the qualities specific to quantities. It is that what is considered from the types of the ten categories is that which falls under only one of them. (And if compounds were considered in the categories and their types, there would result infinite categories) i.e., not limited but very many according to the pairings occurring between them dual, triple, up to ten, and there would also result unlimited types according to possible composition, for example those types among the four divisions of quality (kayf) with each other. And on this basis, khalqah should not be counted among the qualities specific to quantities because it is composed of two different types. But they said: 'Khalqah is only considered and included in this type on the basis of a unity that occurs to the combination of shape (shakl) and color (lawn); according to that unity, a thing is described as beautiful in shape when color accompanies it and there results from them a unitary quality by which beauty and ugliness are said,' meaning that one says of a thing that it is beautiful in form (ḥusn al-ṣūrah) or ugly in form. And these two (beauty and ugliness of form) are different from the beauty and ugliness that attach to shape alone or to color alone. The author said: 'This excuse is not clear, because if they claim that there is a real unity between shape and color, we deny it; and if they are satisfied with a mental unity, then it is permissible to consider it in every two things that come together.' And it may be said: 'Unity between them has been considered in common usage where people express them as khalqah and describe a person by them as beautiful in form or ugly in form; therefore we counted them as one quality and included them in what their parts are included in, as you know, and we have not found a parallel for them in that, so we sufficed with it.'
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a note (tanbih) addressing a problem in classifying khalqah (the combination of shape and color) as a quality (kayfiyyah) specific to quantities. It begins by stating the general rule: each of the ten Aristotelian categories contains only simple types; if compounds were allowed, infinite categories would arise from pairings. Since khalqah is a composition of shape (from the quality of shape) and color (from the quality of color), it should not be counted among the simple qualities specific to quantities. However, the scholars considered khalqah as a single quality on the basis of a conventional unity between shape and color in everyday language, where beauty and ugliness are attributed to the composite (e.g., a person of good form). The author critiques: if they claim a real unity between shape and color, it is false; if they rely on mental unity, then any two things could be unified, making classification arbitrary. Then a defense is offered: common usage treats them as one, and since no other example of such a composite quality exists, they accept this exception.

كم

مقولة
وحدة اعتبارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Khalqah is a simple quality like shape or color alone.
Correction: Actually, khalqah combines shape and color, so it is composite; the rule of categories requires simplicity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if compounds were considered in the categories?
A) Categories would become more precise.
B) Infinite categories would result.
C) Nothing; categories are flexible.
D) Khalqah would be excluded.

Key Takeaway
The ten categories admit only simple types; considering compounds would lead to infinite categories, making khalqah's classification as a single quality problematic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you categorize concepts in your own mind (e.g., what is true, what is good), do you ever settle for a compound label instead of examining the simples? How might that lead you to overlook deeper realities? Commit to identifying one area where you can break down a 'compound' idea into its simple components this week.
?
This lesson warns that infinite categories arise from compounds. In your spiritual life, do you multiply distinctions unnecessarily, creating confusion instead of clarity? Where can you simplify your understanding of a matter of faith (like the attributes of Allah) to avoid endless complications? Resolve to study one attribute without mixing it with others.
?
If khalqah is composite, how much more must your own actions be composite in their motives? Will you examine what 'simple' intentions lie behind your deeds today, or will you let them remain a vague compound? Set a time tonight to review your day's actions and isolate the pure intention from mixed ones.

The Justification of Khalqah as a Single Quality and Its Critique
Al-Sanusi presents the justification for khalqah's classification based on conventional unity, critiques it, and records a defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the justification for treating khalqah as a single quality based on conventional unity, and the author's critique that such unity is either false or too permissive.

Core Idea
Conventional unity can't rescue a flawed classification.

In the previous lesson, we saw why khalqah—being a composite of shape and color—should not be counted among the simple qualities. Yet the scholars did include it. In this passage, al-Sanusi presents their justification: they appealed to a conventional unity between shape and color in everyday usage, giving rise to a single quality described as 'beauty of form' (husn al-surah). He then offers his incisive critique and eventually transmits a defense from the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Read the justification
The scholars claim that khalqah is considered a single quality because of a unity between shape and color that yields descriptions like 'beautiful form'.
Note the phrase 'باعتبار وحدة'.
2
Identify the basis: conventional unity
The unity is not real but conventional—based on how people speak. Beauty/ugliness of form is attributed to the composite, not to shape or color alone.
3
Understand the author's critique
Al-Sanusi argues: if they claim real unity, we deny it; if they claim mental unity, then any two things could be considered one, making classification arbitrary.
This is a powerful dilemma.
4
Examine the counter-defense
A defender says common usage already treats them as one, and there is no parallel for this composite quality, so the exception stands.
The defense relies on uniqueness ('لم نجد لها نظيراً').
5
Reflect on the tension
Consider how logical strictness conflicts with everyday language. Why might the author still find the justification weak?

Definition and Types of Dispositional Qualities
The author defines dispositional qualities and refutes a mistaken addition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what dispositional qualities are, their two main types (weakness and strength), and why active power is not considered a dispositional quality.

Core Idea
Dispositional qualities are either receptive (weakness) or resistive (strength), not active power.

This passage from al-Mawaqif clarifies a key category in the science of qualities (kayfiyyat). The author defines dispositional qualities and distinguishes them from other types. He also corrects a common error where some philosophers mistakenly included active power as a third division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the two types of dispositional qualities presented.
The passage begins with 'I' for either/or.
2
Identify the two true types
Find the two divisions: disposition toward reception (weakness) and disposition toward repulsion (strength). Note the examples: prone to illness (mumarradi) and healthiness (musihhi).
3
Understand the correction
The author then addresses a mistaken third type: power of action. He argues that wrestling involves knowledge, limb hardness, and ability, which are not dispositional qualities.
This shows the precision required in kalam categorization.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you understand why active power is excluded.
Checkpoint: Why is wrestling not a dispositional quality?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ الفصل الرابع ] من فصول الكيف ( في الكيفيات الاستعدادية وهي (إما) استعداد (نحو القبول ) والانفعال ( ويسمى ضعفاً) ولا قوة كالممراضية ( وإما ) استعداد ( نحو الدفع واللاقبول ويسمى قوة ولا ضعفاً) كالمصحاحية ( وإما قوة الفعل كالقوة على المصارعة فليست منها أي من الكيفيات الاستعدادية كما ظنه قوم وجعلوا أقسامها ثلاثة ) فإنَّ المصارعة مثلاً تتعلق بعلم ) بهذه الصناعة ( وصلابة الأعضاء لئلا يتأثر بسرعة ) ولا يمكن عطفها بسهولة (و) تتعلق ( بالقدرة على هذا الفعل ) وشيء منها أي من هذه الثلاثة التي تعلق بها المصارعة (ليس من هذا الجنس) الذي هو الكيفية الاستعدادية، لأنَّ العلم والقدرة من الكيفيات النفسانية وصلابة الأعضاء من الكيفيات الملموسة على ما مر
[ The Fourth Chapter ] of the chapters on quality: (On dispositional qualities, and they are (either) a disposition (toward reception) and passivity, and it is called weakness and not strength, like being prone to illness; (or) a disposition (toward repulsion and non-reception, and it is called strength and not weakness, like healthiness; (or) the power of action, like the strength for wrestling, and it is not from them, i.e., from the dispositional qualities, as some people thought and made its divisions three. For wrestling, for example, involves knowledge of this craft and hardness of limbs so that it is not affected quickly and cannot be bent easily, and it involves the ability to perform this action. None of these three that wrestling involves is of this genus which is dispositional quality, because knowledge and ability are from the psychic qualities, and hardness of limbs is from the tangible qualities, as previously stated.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage classifies dispositional qualities (الكيفيات الاستعدادية). It begins by stating that there are two true types: first, a disposition toward reception and passivity, called weakness (e.g., being prone to illness); second, a disposition toward repulsion and non-reception, called strength (e.g., healthiness). Some mistakenly add a third: the power of action, like strength for wrestling. The author argues that wrestling involves knowledge, physical limb hardness, and ability, which belong to different quality categories (psychic and tangible), not to dispositional qualities. Therefore, the third type is excluded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some consider active power (like strength for wrestling) as a third type of dispositional quality.
Correction: Active power involves knowledge, ability (psychic qualities) and limb hardness (tangible qualities), not disposition.

Checkpoint
Why is the power of action (like strength for wrestling) not considered a dispositional quality according to the author?
A) Because it is not a quality at all.
B) Because it involves knowledge, ability, and physical hardness which belong to other quality types.
C) Because dispositional qualities only exist in inanimate objects.
D) Because the author denies the existence of such power.

Key Takeaway
Dispositional qualities are limited to receptive and resistive capacities, not active abilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever mistaken your active abilities (skills, knowledge) for your inner disposition? How can you tell the difference between what you can do (active power) and what you are inclined to receive or resist (disposition)?
?
Consider your spiritual state: are you more receptive to negative influences (weakness) or do you have a resistive strength that guards your faith? What specific practices could help you strengthen your resistive disposition?
?
The passage warns against confusing categories. In your own life, are there areas where you confuse what you are (disposition) with what you can do (ability)? How can this clarification change your approach to self-improvement?

Can a Single Quality Belong to Multiple Categories?
A conditional argument about overlapping quality categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a quality can belong to multiple categories if the categories are not essentially distinct, as argued in the text.

Core Idea
A single quality can straddle multiple categories if the categories are not essentially distinct.

This passage examines a critical nuance in the classification of qualities. The claim that knowledge and ability are psychic qualities rests on the assumption that quality categories are rigidly separate. But what if they overlap? The author presents a conditional argument: if the categories are not essentially distinct, then a single quality—like ability—can belong to both psychic and dispositional categories. This challenges the initial division and opens the door for overlapping classifications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'based on the supposition that... otherwise...'.
The passage is a single logical argument; try to follow its flow.
2
Identify the two conditions
Notice the two scenarios: (1) categories are essentially distinct, (2) categories are not essentially distinct. The author argues that under the second condition, overlap is possible.
The word 'otherwise' signals the alternative.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the examples given: ability can be both psychic and dispositional; color, straightness, and curvature are both sensible and specific to quantities. Understand how they illustrate the point.
These examples show that categories are not watertight.
4
Consider the implication
Reflect on how this argument challenges a rigid classification of qualities. Why might this be important in philosophy or kalam?
This connects to broader debates about categorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لأن العلم والقدرة من الكيفيات النفسانية قيل: هذا مبني على أن الكيفية المحسوسة المسماة بالانفعالات والانفعاليات والكيفيات النفسانية المسماة بالملكة أو الحال والكيفيات المختصة بالكميات والكيفيات الاستعدادية أقام من الكيف متباينة بالذات يمتنع صدق البعض منها على شيء مما صدق عليه الآخر، وإلا فلا يمتنع أن تكون القدرة من حيث اختصاصها بذوات الأنفس من الكيفيات النفسانية ومن حيث كونها قوة شديدة فاعلة بسهولة من الكيفيات الاستعدادية كما ذكروا أنَّ اللون والاستقامة والانحناء ونحو ذلك من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات مع كونها من المحسوسات .
His saying: (because knowledge and ability are from the psychic qualities. It was said: This is based on the supposition that the sensible quality called passions and passional qualities, and the psychic qualities called disposition or state, and the qualities specific to quantities, and the dispositional qualities are different in essence, such that it is impossible for some of them to be true of something to which another is true. Otherwise, it would not be impossible for ability to be from the psychic qualities in terms of its specificity to souls, and from the dispositional qualities in terms of being a strong power acting with ease, just as they mentioned that color, straightness, curvature, and the like are among the qualities specific to quantities while being among sensible things.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents a critical comment on the earlier classification. It points out that the author's argument that knowledge and ability are psychic qualities is based on the assumption that the different types of quality (sensible, psychic, specific to quantities, dispositional) are essentially distinct. If they are not essentially distinct, then a single quality could belong to multiple categories. For example, ability could be both psychic (since it resides in souls) and dispositional (as a strong power). Similarly, color, straightness, and curvature are both sensible qualities and specific to quantities. This challenges the rigid division of quality categories.

المحسوسات
الانفعالات
الانفعاليات
الملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that a quality can only belong to one category, and that categories are mutually exclusive.
Correction: The text shows that if categories are not essentially distinct, a single quality can belong to multiple categories, as in the examples of ability and color.

Checkpoint
Under what condition could ability (القدرة) be considered both a psychic quality and a dispositional quality?
A) If categories are essentially distinct.
B) If categories are not essentially distinct.
C) If ability is only psychic.
D) If ability is only dispositional.

Key Takeaway
The division of quality categories is not absolute; a single quality can belong to multiple categories if the categories are not essentially distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson shows that categories like 'psychic' and 'dispositional' can overlap for a single quality like ability. In your own life, where have you insisted on rigid categories (e.g., 'sacred' vs 'secular') that might actually overlap? What would change if you allowed for that overlap starting today?
?
The passage argues that overlap is possible only if the categories are not essentially distinct. Consider a belief or practice you hold—such as your view of 'knowledge' and 'action'. Have you assumed they are essentially separate? How does the possibility of overlap challenge you to integrate them more deeply in your daily life?
?
If ability can be both psychic and dispositional, then your own abilities (like knowledge, skill, or character) likely have multiple aspects. Are you neglecting one aspect while focusing on another? Identify one ability you have and commit to nurturing both its inner (psychic) and outwardly effective (dispositional) dimensions this week.

Types of Dispositional Qualities and Exclusion of Power of Action
Classification of istidadiyyah qualities and exclusion of quwwat al-fi'l.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proper types of dispositional qualities (weakness and strength) and why power of action (quwwat al-fi'l) is not included among them.

Core Idea
Dispositional qualities are only about passive or active receptivity, not about active power.

This passage from al-Ījī's Mawāqif classifies the category of dispositional qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-istidādiyya). The author identifies two genuine types—weakness and strength—and then refutes the mistaken addition of a third type, power of action. By understanding this classification, you will grasp an important precision in the ontology of qualities that prevents confusion between different kinds of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Notice the clear division: the first two are proper, the third is excluded.
The passage includes a short commentary on examples.
2
Identify the two proper types
Look for the two dispositions: one toward acceptance/passivity (weakness) and one toward repulsion/activity (strength). Note their examples: mumarāḍiyya and muṣāḥiḥiyya.
3
Understand the refutation of the third type
The author denies that power of action (e.g., wrestling strength) is a dispositional quality. He argues that it involves knowledge, ability, and limb solidity, which are from other quality categories.
Consider why each component belongs elsewhere.
4
Examine the ontological objection and response
The commentary addresses a subtle objection: could ability be both psychic and dispositional? The answer is that the quality categories are essentially distinct, so one item cannot belong to two different categories.
This reinforces the strict separation of quality types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع من فصول الكيف في الكيفيات الاستعدادية وهي إما استعداد نحو القبول والانفعال ويسمى ضعفاً ولا قوة كالممراضية وإما استعداد نحو الدفع واللاقبول ويسمى قوة ولا ضعفاً كالمصحاحية وإما قوة الفعل كالقوة على المصارعة فليست منها أي من الكيفيات الاستعدادية كما ظنه قوم وجعلوا أقسامها ثلاثة فإن المصارعة مثلاً تتعلق بعلم بهذه الصناعة وصلابة الأعضاء لئلا يتأثر بسرعة ولا يمكن عطفها بسهولة وتتعلق بالقدرة على هذا الفعل وشيء منها أي من هذه الثلاثة التي تعلق بها المصارعة ليس من هذا الجنس الذي هو الكيفية الاستعدادية لأن العلم والقدرة من الكيفيات النفسانية وصلابة الأعضاء من الكيفيات الملموسة على ما مر

قوله : كالممراضية رجل ممراض كثير القبول للمرض والممراضية كونه كثيراً بقبول المرض وكذا المصحاحية.

قوله : لأن العلم والقدرة من الكيفيات النفسانية قيل: هذا مبني على أن الكيفية المحسوسة المسماة بالانفعالات والانفعاليات والكيفيات النفسانية المسماة بالملكة أو الحال والكيفيات المختصة بالكميات والكيفيات الاستعدادية أقام من الكيف متباينة بالذات يمتنع صدق البعض منها على شيء مما صدق عليه الآخر وإلا فلا يمتنع أن تكون القدرة من حيث اختصاصها بذوات الأنفس من الكيفيات النفسانية ومن حيث كونها قوة شديدة فاعلة بسهولة من الكيفيات الاستعدادية كما ذكروا أن اللون والاستقامة والانحناء ونحو ذلك من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات مع كونها من المحسوسات.
The fourth chapter of the chapters on quality: on the qualities of disposition. They are either a disposition towards acceptance and passivity, called weakness and not strength, like mumarāḍiyya (being prone to illness); or a disposition towards repulsion and non-acceptance, called strength and not weakness, like muṣāḥiḥiyya (being healthy); or the power of action, like the strength for wrestling. That is not from them, i.e., from the qualities of disposition, as some people thought and made their divisions three. For wrestling, for example, involves knowledge of this craft, and solidity of the limbs so that they are not quickly affected and cannot be easily bent, and it involves the ability to perform this act. None of these three things involved in wrestling is of this kind, which is the dispositional quality, because knowledge and ability are from the psychic qualities, and solidity of the limbs is from the tangible qualities, as previously mentioned.

His saying: "like mumarāḍiyya": a man mumarāḍ is one who frequently accepts illness, and mumarāḍiyya is his being frequent in accepting illness; similarly muṣāḥiḥiyya.

His saying: "because knowledge and ability are from the psychic qualities." It is said: This is based on the fact that the sensible quality called passions and passional qualities, the psychic qualities called disposition or state, the qualities specific to quantities, and the dispositional qualities are essentially distinct categories, such that it is impossible for some of them to apply to something to which another applies. Otherwise, it would not be impossible for ability, in terms of its specificity to souls, to be from the psychic qualities, and in terms of its being a strong power acting with ease, to be from the dispositional qualities, as they mentioned that color, straightness, curvature, and the like are from the qualities specific to quantities while being from the sensibles.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage classifies the qualities of disposition (al-kayfiyyāt al-istidādiyya) and clarifies what properly belongs to them. The author states that these qualities are either a disposition towards acceptance and passivity, which is called weakness (ḍuʿf) and not strength, giving the example of mumarāḍiyya (a state of being prone to illness), or a disposition towards repulsion and non-acceptance, which is called strength (quwwa) and not weakness, giving the example of muṣāḥiḥiyya (a state of being healthy). Some people mistakenly add a third division: the power of action (quwwat al-fiʿl), such as strength for wrestling. The author refutes this by analyzing wrestling: it involves knowledge of the craft, solidity of the limbs (to avoid being easily affected or bent), and the ability to perform the act. None of these three belongs to the genus of dispositional qualities. Knowledge and ability are psychic qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-nafsāniyya), and limb solidity is a tangible quality (min al-kayfiyyāt al-malmūsāt). The commentary then explains the example of mumarāḍiyya and muṣāḥiḥiyya, and addresses an objection: one might argue that ability could be both psychic and dispositional, but the author responds that the categories are essentially distinct; if they were not, then ability could be considered under both categories, but this is not the case, just as color and straightness are qualities specific to quantities while also being sensible.

الكيفيات الملموسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power of action (quwwat al-fi'l) is a type of dispositional quality.
Correction: Power of action involves knowledge, ability, and bodily qualities, which are psychic or tangible, not dispositional. Dispositional qualities only concern passive or active receptivity, not the power to act.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT considered a proper type of al-kayfiyyat al-istidadiyyah according to the passage?
A) al-ḍuʿf (weakness)
B) al-quwwa (strength)
C) quwwat al-fiʿl (power of action)

Key Takeaway
Dispositional qualities are only of two types: weakness (passive receptivity) and strength (active resistance); power of action is excluded because its components belong to other quality categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you often rely on your own 'power of action' (quwwat al-fi'l) in daily tasks, forgetting that knowledge, ability, and even physical strength are gifts from Allah? This lesson reminds us that these are not inherent dispositional qualities but rather psychic and tangible qualities from Allah. How will you consciously acknowledge His grant of these before and after every action today?
?
Consider your personal 'weaknesses' (ḍu'f), like a tendency to sickness or a short temper. Do you accept these as part of Allah's decree with patience, or do you complain? The lesson teaches that such dispositions are natural qualities—how can you use this understanding to increase your tawakkul and gratitude for health, recognizing that both weakness and strength are from Him?
?
The lesson argues that the categories of quality are distinct and cannot be confused. Are you intellectually rigorous in distinguishing between spiritual states (psychic qualities) and physical dispositions? For instance, do you sometimes attribute spiritual significance to physical traits or vice versa? What step can you take today to avoid such category errors in your own reasoning about faith and life?

The Disagreement Over Relative Categories
Sanusi introduces the fourth observation on relations and states the disagreement over relative categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key disagreement between philosophers and theologians regarding the existence of relative categories, and which category the theologians accept.

Core Idea
Theologians reject all relative categories except one: al-ayn.

The study of relative categories (al-maqūlāt al-nisbiyya) is central to Islamic metaphysics. This passage introduces the fundamental divide between the philosophers, who affirm these categories as real, and the theologians, who limit their acceptance to only the category of place/time (al-ayn). Understanding this disagreement sets the stage for the arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the direct statement of disagreement.
2
Identify the two camps
Note that philosophers (al-ḥukamā') affirm all relative categories, while theologians (al-mutakallimūn) deny them except for al-ayn.
3
Understand the exception
Realize that al-ayn is a relative category that theologians accept; they acknowledge its existence in the external world.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this disagreement matters for theological and philosophical arguments about existence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع من مراصد الموقف الثالث في النسب أي المقولات النسبية وفيه مقدمة لبيان أنها موجودة في الخارج أو لا وفصلان لبيان مباحث ما اتفق على وجوده أعني الأين تارة على رأي المتكلمين وتارة على رأي الحكماء. المقدمة : الخلاف على إثبات المقولات أثبت الحكماء المقولات النسبية وأنكرها المتكلمون إلا الأين فإنهم اعترفوا بوجوده وأنكروا وجود ما عداه منها. لوجوه الأول : لو وجدت الأعراض النسبية لزم التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة أما أولا فلأن هذه الأعراض لا بد لها من محل ولا شك أن محلها يتصف بها فله إليها نسبة بالمحلية والاتصاف وهذه النسبة موجودة أيضاً على ذلك التقدير ويعود الكلام فيها بأن يقال هذه النسبة أيضاً لها محل يتصف بها فله إليها نسبة ثالثة موجودة وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له فهذا تسلسل وأما ثانياً فلان لوجودها الزائد على ماهيتها لما مر إليها نسبة هي

قوله : وفيه مقدمة المراد بالمقدمة ما يجب تقديمه على المباحث المتعلقة بكل واحد

قوله لبيان مباحث ما اتفق على وجوده أي مباحث لها نوع تعلق سواء كانت من عوارضها أو من عوارض ما يتوقف به وإنما قلنا ذلك لأن المباحث التي الفصل الثاني مباحث الحركة عند الحكماء وهي ليست باين عندهم لكنها قد تقع في الاين فللمباحث نوع تعلق به . قوله: لزم التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة بخلاف ما إذا كانت أموراً اعتبارية ثابتة في نفس الأمر لأن اللازم حينئذ يكون مبدأ انتزاعها موجوداً فيه لا وجودها مفصلة فإن وجودها التفصيلي بحسب اعتبار العقل فافهم فإنه قد زل فيه بعض الأقدام .

قوله: لما مر من أن وجود الممكنات زائد على ماهيتها.

قوله : لزم التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة قيل : لا شك أن هذه النسب ليست باعتبارية فرضية بل حقيقية لها تحقق في محالها بمعنى اتصاف محالها بها في نفس الأمر فيلزم التسلسل في الاعتبارية الحقيقية وأنه باطل كالتسلسل في الأمور الموجودة والجواب منع بطلانه كيف وبرهان التطبيق إنما يجري في الموجودات باتفاق الفريقين إنما الخلاف في اشتراط الترتيب والاجتماع في الوجود ولا دليل آخر يجري هاهنا وإلا اقتضى خلو هذه النسب عن الوجود والعدم لجريان الإبطال في كل منهما والعقل قاض ببطلانه .
The fourth observation from the third station: on relations, i.e., the relative categories. It contains an introduction to clarify whether they exist externally or not, and two chapters to clarify the investigations of what is agreed to exist, namely al-ayn, once according to the theologians’ view and once according to the philosophers’ view. The introduction: The disagreement on affirming the categories. The philosophers affirmed the relative categories, while the theologians denied them except for al-ayn, for they acknowledged its existence and denied the existence of what is other than it. According to proofs: The first: If relative accidents existed, it would necessitate infinite regress in existing things. First: Because these accidents must have a subject, and there is no doubt that their subject is qualified by them, so it has a relation to them of subject-hood and qualification. This relation also exists on that assumption, and the argument recurs: it is said that this relation also has a subject qualified by it, so it has to it a third existing relation, and so on ad infinitum—this is a regress. Second: Because their existence is additional to their quiddity, as previously mentioned, they have a relation to it...

His saying: "and it [the introduction] contains an introduction"—meaning by introduction that which must be presented before the investigations concerning each one.

His saying: "to clarify the investigations of what is agreed to exist"—that is, investigations that have some connection, whether they are from its accidentals or from the accidentals of what depends on it. And we only said that because the investigations in the second chapter are investigations of motion according to the philosophers, and it is not al-ayn for them, but it may occur in al-ayn, so the investigations have some connection to it.

His saying: "infinite regress in existing things"—contrary to the case if they were mental considerations established in themselves, because then the necessary [relation] would be a principle of their derivation existing in it, not their detailed existence. For their detailed existence is according to the consideration of the intellect. So understand, for some feet have slipped here.

His saying: "as previously mentioned that the existence of contingents is additional to their quiddities."

His saying: "infinite regress in existing things." It is said: There is no doubt that these relations are not hypothetical mental considerations but real, having realization in their subjects in the sense that their subjects are qualified by them in themselves. Then infinite regress would follow in the real mental considerations, and it is false just like the regress in existing things. The response is to deny its falsity. How so? The proof of correlation (burhān al-taṭbīq) only applies to existents by agreement of both groups; the disagreement is only about the condition of order and coexistence in existence. There is no other proof that applies here; otherwise it would necessitate that these relations be free of existence and non-existence, because the refutation would apply in each case, and the intellect judges that to be false.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage introduces the debate between philosophers (al-ḥukamā’) and theologians (al-mutakallimūn) over the existence of relative categories (al-maqūlāt al-nisbiyya). The author states that philosophers affirm all relative categories, while theologians deny them all except for one: al-ayn (the category of place/time). The theologians acknowledge the existence of al-ayn but deny the rest. The first proof against the relative categories is presented: if relative accidents existed, it would lead to an infinite regress. The argument has two parts. First, every relative accident requires a subject that is qualified by it, giving rise to a relation of subject-hood between them. This relation would itself be a relative accident requiring another subject, leading to a third relation, and so on ad infinitum. Second, because the existence of these accidents is additional to their quiddity (māhiyya), they would have a relation to that added existence, again leading to regress. The commentary then explains various points: the meaning of 'introduction', the scope of investigations connected to al-ayn, and a clarification that the regress only applies to mind-dependent entities if they are considered real and not merely mental. Finally, an objection is raised: if these relations are real and not merely hypothetical, then the regress would apply to them as well, but the response denies the falsity of such regress, arguing that the proof of correlation (burhān al-taṭbīq) only applies to existents by consensus, and the disagreement lies in the condition of order and coexistence. Without further proof, the relations would be neither existent nor non-existent, which is absurd.

Key Terms
المقولات النسبية
الأين
المتكلمون
الحكماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All relative categories are denied by theologians.
Correction: Theologians accept al-ayn (the category of place/time) and deny only the others.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which relative category do the theologians (mutakallimun) accept?
A) al-ayn (place/time)
B) al-mata (when)
C) al-wad' (position)

Key Takeaway
The theologians affirm the reality of one relative category, al-ayn, but reject the rest, unlike the philosophers who accept all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson presents a sharp disagreement among scholars about what exists. Is there a core belief you hold that you have never critically examined, simply because it is not debated in your circles? Would you be willing to test it as these scholars tested relative categories?
?
The theologians made an exception for al-ayn based on reasoning. Are there areas in your life where you accept a truth selectively—perhaps because it is convenient or familiar—while denying others without rigorous thought? How can you emulate the intellectual honesty of these scholars?

The Infinite Regress Argument Against Relative Accidents
Sanusi presents the first argument against relative accidents: if they exist, they cause infinite regress in two ways.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof offered by theologians against relative accidents, showing that they lead to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
If relative accidents existed, each would require a subject, leading to an endless chain.

Having established the disagreement, the author now presents the first proof against the existence of relative categories other than al-ayn. The argument uses the impossibility of infinite regress to show that if relative accidents were real, they would generate an infinite series of relations. This proof is foundational in Islamic kalam against the reality of many relative categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Carefully read the Arabic and translation of the first proof. Note that it has two distinct parts (firstly and secondly).
2
Understand the first part
The accident requires a subject qualified by it, creating a relation that itself is a relative accident, leading to an infinite chain.
3
Understand the second part
The accident's existence is additional to its quiddity, creating a relation to that existence, again leading to regress.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that this regress proves such relative accidents cannot exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

First, every relative accident requires a subject that is qualified by it, giving rise to a relation of subject-hood between them. This relation would itself be a relative accident requiring another subject, leading to a third relation, and so on ad infinitum. Second, because the existence of these accidents is additional to their quiddity (māhiyya), they would have a relation to that added existence, again leading to regress. The commentary then explains various points: the meaning of 'introduction', the scope of investigations connected to al-ayn, and a clarification that the regress only applies to mind-dependent entities if they are considered real and not merely mental. Finally, an objection is raised: if these relations are real and not merely hypothetical, then the regress would apply to them as well, but the response denies the falsity of such regress, arguing that the proof of correlation (burhān al-taṭbīq) only applies to existents by consensus, and the disagreement lies in the condition of order and coexistence. Without further proof, the relations would be neither existent nor non-existent, which is absurd.

Key Terms
التسلسل
الأعراض النسبية
المحلية
الاتصاف
الوجود
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consequence claimed if relative accidents existed?
A) Infinite regress in existing things
B) Contradiction in the divine attributes
C) The category of al-ayn would be impossible

Key Takeaway
The first proof against relative accidents is that their existence leads to an infinite regress, which is deemed impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your relationship with Allah, do you ever find yourself adding unnecessary intermediaries or rationalizations that lead to an endless chain of 'ifs' and 'buts'? What foundational truth must you hold to stop that regress?
?
The argument shows that multiplying relations leads to absurdity. Are there any 'relative accidents' in your spiritual life—habits, doubts, or excuses—that you keep adding between yourself and sincere worship? How will you eliminate them starting today?
?
This lesson forces you to confront infinite regress in thought. When you reflect on your own beliefs about Allah's attributes, are you content with the simplicity of tawheed, or do you unconsciously add complexity? What step can you take to purify your understanding?

The Infinite Regress Argument Against Relative Things
Al-Iji enumerates four infinite regresses from assuming relations exist externally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji uses multiple infinite regresses to disprove the external existence of relative things.

Core Idea
Relations cannot exist externally because they generate endless chains of new relations.

Al-Iji presents the first of several arguments against the reality of relative things (al-nisab). He shows that if relations were real entities, they would lead to not one but four distinct infinite regresses. This lesson walks through each regress step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first regress
Read the initial part: if a thing is described by existence, that description implies a relation to itself, and that relation would require another relation, ad infinitum.
This is the first regress from self-relation.
2
Understand the second regress
If the relation itself exists, its existence requires a third relation to it, and so on. This is a second regress.
3
Understand the third and fourth regresses
For time parts, if priority and posteriority are real, then priority itself becomes prior to posteriority, generating a regress of priorities (third) and posteriorities (fourth).
The passage distinguishes between the regress of priorities and that of posteriorities.
4
Grasp the overall argument
Realize that the impossibility of infinite regress refutes the external existence of relations. Each regress is a distinct reductio ad absurdum.
The argument targets the notion that relations are extra-mental entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتصافها بالوجود، وهذه النسبة أيضاً موجودة على ذلك التقدير فلوجودها إليها نسبة ثالثة وهكذا وهذا تسلسل ثان ( وأما ثالثاً : فلان لأجزاء الزمان بعضها إلى بعض نسبة ) بالتقدم والتأخر ، فلو كانت النسب موجودة في الأعيان لكان التقدم والتأخر موجودين مع موصوفيهما وما مع المتقدم مقدم فيكون التقدم الموجود مع الزمان المتقدم متقدماً على التأخر الموجود مع الزمان المتأخر، فللتقدم تقدم آخر وهكذا للتأخر تأخر آخر فهناك تسلسل ثالث بل رابع أيضاً . قوله : (بل رابع أيضاً) وهو تسلسل التأخرات . قوله: (تسلسل ثالث بل رابع أيضاً) التسلسل الثالث بالنظر إلى التقدم والرابع بالنظر إلى التأخر.
Its being described by existence, and this relation also exists on that assumption, so for its existence there is a third relation to it, and so on. This is a second regress. As for the third: because the parts of time have a relation to one another of priority and posteriority. So if the relations existed in actual entities, then priority and posteriority would exist with their subjects, and what is with the prior is prior. Thus the priority that exists with prior time would be prior to the posteriority that exists with posterior time. So for priority there is another priority, and likewise for posteriority another posteriority. Thus there is a third regress, indeed a fourth. He said: (rather a fourth also) – it is the regress of posteriorities. He said: (a third and a fourth regress) – the third regress is with respect to priority, and the fourth with respect to posteriority.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a proof by reduction to absurdity (burhān al-tasalsul) against the existence of relative things (al-nisab) as real external entities. The author, al-Iji, presents the first face (al-wajh al-awwal) of the argument. He begins with an initial sequence: if a thing has a relation to itself by being described with existence, then that relation itself would require another relation, leading to an infinite regress (first regress). Then he mentions a second regress: if the relation exists, its existence requires a third relation, and so on. The third regress is based on the temporal parts (ajzā' al-zamān) having relations of priority and posteriority. If these relations existed externally, then the priority in prior time would itself be prior to the posteriority in posterior time, generating a new priority for each priority (third regress) and a new posteriority for each posteriority (fourth regress). The glosses clarify that the third regress is by consideration of priority and the fourth by consideration of posteriority. Thus, assuming external existence of relations leads to infinite regress, which is impossible.

Key Terms
النسب (al-nisab)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
التقدم (al-taqaddum)
التأخر (al-ta'akhkhur)
الأجزاء الزمانية (ajza' al-zaman)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only presents one infinite regress.
Correction: Al-Iji distinguishes between four distinct regresses: from self-relation, from existence of relation, from priority, and from posteriority.

Checkpoint
How many distinct regresses does al-Iji identify in this passage?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Assuming external relations leads to multiple impossible infinite regresses: self-relation, existence-relation, priority, and posteriority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that assuming external relations leads to absurd infinite chains. In your own life, do you sometimes treat relative matters (e.g., status, possessions) as absolute, as if they exist independently? How does this argument challenge you to reconsider what is truly real?
?
The impossibility of infinite regress implies that there must be a foundation. Have you ever tried to ground your faith or values on an endless series of reasons? What would it mean for you to rest on a first, uncaused foundation?

Second Argument: Addition Requires Simultaneous Existence of Relata
Al-Iji presents the second face: if relations exist, then addition requires simultaneous existence of relata, leading to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against the external existence of relations, which shows that addition (al-iḍāfa) would require contradictory coexistence of prior and posterior parts of time.

Core Idea
Real addition forces prior and posterior to coexist — an absurdity.

The second argument builds on the concept of addition (al-iḍāfa), a type of relation. Al-Iji argues that if relations exist externally, then addition must exist. But addition demands that its two relata exist simultaneously. Among the kinds of addition are priority and posteriority. This leads to a contradiction: prior and posterior parts of time would have to exist at the same moment. Because that is impossible, the initial assumption that relations exist externally is false. This lesson unpacks the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Note the structure: a conditional premise, definition of addition, then the sub-argument about time.
The text includes a gloss; treat it as part of the argument.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on 'al-iḍāfa', 'al-muntasibān', 'al-taqaddum', 'al-ta'akhkhur', and 'ajza' al-zaman'. Understand their role in the argument.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the argument: if relations exist → addition exists → addition requires coexistence → priority/posteriority are additions → therefore prior/posterior must coexist → impossible.
This is the core of the reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the impossibility
Why is it impossible for prior and posterior to coexist? Because time parts are sequential; they cannot be simultaneous. This contradiction falsifies the premise.
Consider how this supports the position that relations are mental constructs.
5
Summarize the argument
In your own words, restate the argument using the key terms. Ensure the logical flow is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني لو وجدت ) النسب ( لوجدت (الإضافة لأنها من النسب لكونها نسبة متكررة ( وهي لا تتحقق إلا بوجود ) المنتسبين) مجتمعين، ومن أقسام الإضافة التقدم والتأخر فيوجد المتقدم والمتأخر) من أجزاء الزمان (معاً) وإنه باطل قطعاً. قوله : (مجتمعين) العروضها لكل واحد منها بالقياس إلى الآخر.
The second face: if the relations existed, then addition (al-iḍāfa) would exist because it is from among the relations, being a repeated relation. And it is not realized except by the existence of the two relata together. Among the divisions of addition are priority and posteriority. Thus the prior and the posterior from among the parts of time would exist simultaneously, and that is absolutely false. He said: (together) – their being present for each one of them in relation to the other.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is the second argument against the external existence of relations. The author argues that if relations (al-nisab) existed externally, then 'addition' (al-iḍāfa) would also exist, because addition is a type of relation, specifically a repeated relation. Addition requires both relata (al-muntasibān) to exist together (mujtamiʿayn). Among the kinds of addition are priority (al-taqaddum) and posteriority (al-ta'akhkhur). If these are real relations, then the prior and posterior parts of time would have to exist simultaneously. But that is impossible because prior and posterior cannot coexist. The gloss explains that 'together' means the relation is present for each relatum in respect to the other.

Key Terms
الإضافة (al-iḍāfa)
المنتسبان (al-muntasibān)
التقدم (al-taqaddum)
التأخر (al-ta'akhkhur)
الأجزاء الزمانية (ajza' al-zaman)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think addition can exist without simultaneous existence of relata.
Correction: The author asserts that addition is not realized except by the existence of the two relata together (mujtamiʿayn).

Checkpoint
Why does the second argument conclude that priority and posteriority cannot be real relations?
A) Because they are not relations at all
B) Because they would require prior and posterior to exist simultaneously, which is impossible
C) Because time is an illusion
D) Because relations are mental constructs

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that real addition would make prior and posterior coexist, a logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You habitually think of your past sins as 'prior' and your repentance as 'posterior'. If the argument is correct, both cannot exist together in external reality. How does this force you to re-evaluate the reality of time and your reliance on Allah's forgiveness rather than on temporal sequence? Will you now focus more on the quality of your repentance than on its timing?
?
Consider your relationship with Allah: you often describe His mercy as 'preceding' His wrath. If that relation were externally real, then both would coexist eternally. Does that make you less fearful of His wrath or more hopeful of His mercy? How should this argument deepen your awareness that all relations are mental constructs, and true reality is only Allah and His attributes?
?
You plan your day with priorities—prayer first, then work. If priority and posteriority were real relations, then your prayer and work would have to exist simultaneously, collapsing the distinction. Does this not show how dependent your sense of order is on your mind? Will you now strive to make every moment an act of worship, knowing that the order is only in your perception?

The Third Argument: God Would Be Subject to Accidents
Al-Iji presents the third face: if relations are external, God would have changing relations with creatures, implying accidents in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why external relations would entail accidents in God and how some early theologians attempted to avoid the regress.

Core Idea
If God had external relations, He would change — but the Eternal cannot change.

This third argument strikes at the heart of the debate over relations: if they are real externals, then God's relationship with creation would be constantly changing. Al-Iji shows that such change would force God to be subject to accidents, which is impossible. He then notes a historical position that accepted this consequence despite its absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the passage once to grasp the structure: the conditional (if external relations), then the implication (God would have three types of relation to each event), then the problem (the prior relation ceases, but the Eternal cannot cease).
The three relations are: coexistence, priority, and posteriority.
2
Identify the regress
Notice how Maʿmar's acceptance of infinite accidents leads to a vicious regress. The proof of application (burhān al-taṭbīq) refutes it.
Maʿmar affirmed relative accidents but could not solve the regress.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this argument is decisive against the external existence of relations and how it protects divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الثالث: لو وجدت ) النسب في الخارج لزم اتصاف الباري تعالى بالحوادث لأنَّ له مع كل حادث إضافة ثابتة ) إليه ( بأنه موجود معه و ) له (قبله ) أي قبل كل حادث إضافة أخرى إليه ( بأنه متقدم عليه و ) له ( بعده ( إضافة ثالثة إِليه ( بأنه متأخر عنه ( وهذه الإضافة حادثة، أما التي مع الحادث أو بعده فلا شبهة في حدوثها، وأما التي قبله فقد زالت حال وجوده والقديم لا يزول ) وأثبتها ) أي الأعراض النسبية (ضرار) والصواب كما في المحصل معمر، فإنه من قدماء المتكلمين لما رأى قوة الحجة التي ذكرها الحكماء على وجودها إذ عن لها وحكم بوجودها (و) حيث لم يجد دفعاً للتسلسلات المذكورة التزم التسلسل ومن ثم أثبت أعراضاً غير متناهية ) يقوم بعضها ببعض ولا مخلص له من برهان التطبيق . قوله : (حادثة) أي على تقدير وجودها في الخارج. قوله : ( والقديم لما ثبت من أن ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه.
The third face: if the relations existed externally, then the Exalted Creator would necessarily be described by accidents. For He has with every event a fixed addition to it, namely that He exists with it; and before every event another addition to it, namely that He is prior to it; and after it a third addition to it, namely that He is posterior to it. And this addition is accidental (ḥāditha). As for that which is with the event or after it, there is no doubt about its being originated. As for that which is before it, it has ceased at the time of its existence. And the Eternal cannot cease. And he affirmed them (i.e., the relative accidents): Ḍirār, and the correct view is that it is Maʿmar, as in al-Muḥaṣṣal. For he was among the early theologians when he saw the strength of the argument that the philosophers mentioned for their existence, he approved of them and judged their existence. And when he found no way to repel the mentioned regresses, he accepted the regress and accordingly affirmed infinite accidents, one subsisting in another, and he has no escape from the proof of application (burhān al-taṭbīq). He said: (accidental) i.e., on the assumption of its external existence. He said: (and the Eternal) because it is established that what is established as eternal is impossible to cease.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the external existence of relations, then mentions a historical position that accepts them despite the regress. The argument: if relations are external, then God would have changing relations to each created thing – e.g., coexistence with each new creature, priority before it, and posteriority after it. These relations would be originated (ḥāditha). The relation that is before the creature ceases when the creature exists, but the Eternal cannot undergo cessation. Therefore, external relations would imply accidents in God, which is absurd. The author then notes that Ḍirār and Maʿmar (the correct attribution) affirmed relative accidents (aʿrāḍ nisbiyya). Maʿmar, when he saw the strength of the philosophers' argument for their existence, accepted it, but since he could not refute the regress arguments, he accepted the regress and posited infinite accidents subsisting in each other. He is criticized for this because the proof of application (burhān al-taṭbīq) defeats him. The glosses clarify that 'accidental' means on the assumption of existence, and 'the Eternal' cannot cease.

الحوادث (al-ḥawādith)
الإضافة (al-iḍāfa)
القديم (al-qadīm)

برهان التطبيق (burhān al-taṭbīq)
ضرار (Ḍirār)
معمر (Maʿmar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's relations (like being prior to creation) are real and external entities.
Correction: This argument shows that such relations cannot be external because they would entail change in God, which is impossible for the Eternal.
