Philosophers' Argument from Necessary Knowledge and the Mutakallimun's Response
Al-Iji presents the philosophers' argument from necessary knowledge and the response using non-existent attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument for the external existence of relative things and the mutakallimun's response based on non-existent attributes and description theory.

Core Idea
Necessity of knowledge does not prove external existence of relations.

In this passage, Al-Iji presents a key debate: philosophers argue that we necessarily perceive relations like 'above' and 'facing' as external realities. The mutakallimun counter by pointing to non-existent attributes—like blindness—which are attributed to things without being real. This exchange exposes a central difference in how each school understands existence and description.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the opening claim that relations like 'sky above earth' are known necessarily. Consider what 'necessary' means here.
The philosophers rely on universal agreement on such perceptions.
2
Understand the initial objection
The objector denies that such necessity proves external existence. The very existence of relations is disputed.
This shows the mutakallimun do not accept the premise.
3
Grasp the key response: non‑existent attributes
Even if the sky is described as 'above' externally, it does not entail the existence of above-ness as a real thing. The example of Zayd being blind illustrates that attributes can be non‑existent.
This is the core of the mutakallimun's position.
4
Examine the additional argument and its rebuttal
The philosophers argue that if something becomes above after not being above, the new property must be real. The response: 'becoming above' is merely a change in description, not a new existent.
The rebuttal reinforces the idea that relations are mental or abstract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الحكماء على وجود الأمور النسبية ( بأن كون السماء فوق الأرض ومقابلة الشمس لوجه الأرض وأمثالهما من النسب (مما نعلمه ضرورة) . ولقائل أن يقول : إن ادعيتم أن الفوقية مثلاً من الموجودات الخارجية منعناه، بل هذا هو المتنازع فيه، فكيف يدعى الضرورة فيه وإن قلتم : السماء موصوفة بالفوقية في الخارج، فلذلك لا يستلزم وجود الفوقية فيه لجواز اتصاف الأعيان الخارجية بالأمور العدمية، فإنَّ زيداً أعمى في الخارج وليس العمى موجوداً خارجياً، وقد يستدل على ذلك أيضاً بأنَّ الشيء، قد لا يكون فوقاً ثم يصير فوقاً، فالفوقية التي حصلت بعد العدم لا تكون عدمية وإلا كان نفي النفي نفيا وهو محال، ويجاب عنه : بأن حصول الفوقية بعد ما لم تكن عبارة عن اتصاف الشيء بها بعد ما لم يكن متصفاً، وذلك لا يستلزم وجودها كما عرفت . قوله : ( مما تعلمه ضرورة) إن حمل الضرورة على البداهة يكون حاصل الكلام الاستدلال على وجود الأمور النسبية من حيث هي نسبية بوجود الفوقية والمقابلة من حيث خصوصها فدعوى الضرورة حينئذ لا ينافي القول بالاحتجاج للاختلاف في العنوان . قوله : ( لجواز اتصاف إلخ) فإن الاتصاف الخارجي بمعنى ما يكون الخارج ظرفاً النفسه نوعان يستدعى وجود الموصوف والصفة في الخارج كالاتصاف بالسواد وانتزاعي يستدعي وجود المنتزع عنه في الخارج لا وجود المنتزع .
And the philosophers argued for the existence of relative things by saying that the sky being above the earth, and the sun facing the surface of the earth, and similar relations are among the things we know necessarily. But someone may say: if you claim that, for example, above-ness is among external existents, we deny that. Rather, this is the point of dispute. So how can necessity be claimed in it? And if you say: the sky is described by above-ness externally, then that does not entail the existence of above-ness in it, because it is permissible for external entities to be described by non-existential things. For Zayd is blind externally, but blindness is not an external existent. And they also argue for that by saying: a thing may not be above and then becomes above. So the above-ness that occurs after non-existence cannot be non-existential, otherwise the negation of negation would be a negation, which is impossible. It is answered: the occurrence of above-ness after it was not is an expression of the thing being described by it after it was not described, and that does not entail its existence, as you have known. He said: (of what we know necessarily) – if necessity is interpreted as self-evident, then the conclusion of the speech is inference to the existence of relative things qua relative by the existence of above-ness and facing as particular, so the claim of necessity then does not contradict the argument because of the difference in viewpoint. He said: (for the possibility of being described, etc.) – for external description in the sense of the external being a locus for the thing itself is of two types: one requires the existence of the subject and the attribute externally, like being described by blackness; and an abstractive type requires the existence of that from which it is abstracted externally, not the existence of the abstracted.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument for the existence of relative things and the mutakallimun's response. The philosophers claim that we necessarily know relations like 'the sky is above the earth' or 'the sun faces the earth' – these are undeniable perceptions. The objector replies: this is the very point of dispute; you cannot claim necessity for the existence of above-ness as an external entity. The fact that the sky is described as 'above' externally does not prove that above-ness is an external existent, because external entities can be described by non-existential attributes (umūr ʿadamiyya). Example: Zayd is blind, but blindness is not an external something. Then a further argument is presented: if a thing becomes above after not being above, the arising property cannot be non-existential, because that would be a negation of negation implying affirmation. The response: the 'occurrence' of above-ness simply means the thing becomes described by it; it does not entail the existence of an extra attribute. The glosses clarify: the philosophers' argument relies on necessary knowledge, but the mutakallimun reinterpret it as knowledge of the particular rather than the universal. The gloss on external description distinguishes between real description (like blackness) and abstractive description (like blindness), so external description does not imply external existence of the attribute.

Key Terms
الحكماء (al-ḥukamā')
الضرورة (al-ḍarūra)
الأمور العدمية (al-umūr al-ʿadamiyya)
الفوقية (al-fawqiyya)
المقابلة (al-muqābala)
الاتصاف (al-ittiṣāf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that necessary perception of a relation proves its external existence.
Correction: The response shows that description can be of a non‑entity (e.g., blindness), so necessity does not entail external existence.

Checkpoint
How does the mutakallimun respond to the argument that something becomes above after not being above?
A) They deny that things change
B) They say the above-ness is created by God ex nihilo
C) They say it is merely a description of the thing, not an external existent
D) They say it is a mental construct

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun argue that being described by a relation does not require the relation to exist externally, just as blindness is attributed without existing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers insisted that your clear knowledge of a relation proves it is real outside your mind. Think of your relationship with Allah—you know it is real, but do you treat it as a separate 'thing' that exists on its own, or as a description of your state that depends entirely on Him? Which view makes you more grateful and reliant on Him?
?
You often describe yourself with negative attributes like 'I am weak' or 'I am helpless.' The mutakallimun say such descriptions are real but not existing entities. If your weakness is not a 'thing' inside you, but merely a description of a lack, how does that change your strategy for overcoming it and your need for Allah's strength?
?
When your situation changes—like from poverty to wealth—you might think a new 'thing' (wealth) has come into existence. But the lesson teaches that such a change is only a new description of you, not a new entity. If that is true, what does it mean for your attachment to what you have? Is it easier to let go of descriptions than of 'things'?

Classification of Relations into External and Mental
Al-Iji classifies relations into external and mental types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muslim theologians classify relations into those that exist externally and those that are merely invented by the mind.

Core Idea
Not all relations are real; some exist externally, others are mental inventions.

Al-Iji responds to opponents who argued that all relations are mental. He shows that they only prove that not all relations are external, and he then classifies relations into two kinds: those that truly exist externally (like above-ness) and those that the mind invents (like priority). This classification is central to Islamic metaphysics and logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how al-Iji responds to the opponent's argument and then gives his classification.
The passage includes several glosses; focus on the main argument first.
2
Identify the two kinds of relations
Find the two categories: (1) relations that exist externally, like above-ness and facing; (2) relations invented by the mind, like priority and posteriority.
The text says the first kind terminates at a limit, while the second does not.
3
Understand the criterion
Why are some relations external? Because they involve actual relations between real entities. Why are others mental? They are abstractions from comparisons, like priority between two moments.
4
Reflect on the objection
Consider the objection raised in the commentary: the philosophers' proof might apply even to mental relations. Al-Iji's specification might be arbitrary.
This shows the depth of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجابوا عن أدلة الخصم بأنها إنما تنفي كون جميع النسب موجودة في الخارج أي هذه الأدلة تدل على سلب الموجبة الكلية ونحن نقول به فإنَّ من الإضافات والنسب ( أموراً موجودة في الخارج حقيقتها أنها إضافة ( كالفوقية والمقابلة ونظائرهما ) ومنها إضافات ) لا تحقق لها الخارج بل ( يخترعها العقل عند ملاحظة أمرين كالتقدم والتأخر ) بين أمرين لا يجوز اجتماعهما كأجزاء الزمان (و) القسم (الأول) من هذين ( ينتهي عند حد ) أي يجب انتهاؤه إلى حد لا يتجاوزه ) دون الثاني ) إذ لا يقف عند حد لا يمكن للعقل . قوله : ( حقيقتها أنها إضافة لم يظهر لي فائدة هذه المقدمة . قوله : ( يخترعها العقل) أي يعتبره وينتزعه عن أمور موجودة في الخارج ولولا الانتزاع لم تكن تلك الإضافات موجودة بل مبدأ انتزاعها كمية الواجب وقبليته وبعديته وكالحلول والاتصاف . قوله: (ونحن نقول به فإنَّ من الإضافات إلخ) أورد عليه أن دليل الحكماء على تقدير تمامه يدل على وجود تلك النسب أيضا، إذ يقال : اتصاف المحال بها وكذا معية الباري وقبليته وبعديته إلى غير ذلك أمور حاصلة من غير فرض فارض واعتبار معتبر فتخصيصه بما ذكره اعتراف بالتخلف وأنه يوجب البطلان . قوله : ( حقيقتها أنها إضافة الظاهر أنه لا دخل له في المقصود.
And they answered the proofs of the opponent that they only negate all relations being existent externally, i.e., these proofs indicate the negation of the universal affirmative, and we affirm that. For among additions and relations are things existing externally, whose reality is that they are addition, like above-ness and facing and their likes. And among them are additions that have no realization externally; rather, the mind invents them when observing two things, like priority and posteriority between two things that cannot coexist, such as parts of time. And the first of these two divisions terminates at a limit, i.e., it must terminate at a limit it does not exceed, unlike the second, for it does not stop at a limit that the mind cannot exceed. He said: (whose reality is that they are addition) – I do not see the benefit of this premise. He said: (the mind invents them) – i.e., it considers and abstracts them from things existing externally; were it not for the abstraction, those additions would not exist; rather, the source of their abstraction is the quantity of the Necessary, His priority and posteriority, and like inherence and description. He said: (and we affirm that, for among additions etc.) – it is objected that the proof of the philosophers, assuming its completeness, indicates the existence of those relations as well; for it is said: the description of impossible things with them, and likewise the with-ness of the Creator, His priority and posteriority, and so on, are things obtained without any supposing and considering. So his specification to what he mentioned is an admission of non-conformity and that it necessitates invalidity. He said: (whose reality is that they are addition) – apparently it has no bearing on the intended.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents the resolution: the opponents' arguments only negate that all relations exist externally, but we affirm that some do. The classification: (1) relations that exist externally, whose reality is addition (iḍāfa), such as above-ness (al-fawqiyya) and facing (al-muqābala) and their likes. (2) Relations that have no external reality; the mind invents them when considering two things, like priority and posteriority between things that cannot coexist (like parts of time). The first kind terminates at a limit; the second can be extended indefinitely. The glosses critique some points: one gloss questions the benefit of the premise 'their reality is addition'. Another gloss explains 'the mind invents' as abstraction from externals. A further gloss notes an objection: the philosophers' proof would also apply to these mental relations, so the classification is arbitrary. The final gloss says the premise 'their reality is addition' is irrelevant.

Key Terms
الإضافات (al-iḍāfāt)
المخترعة العقلية (al-mukhtaraʿa al-ʿaqliyya)
الفوقية (al-fawqiyya)
المقابلة (al-muqābala)
التقدم (al-taqaddum)
التأخر (al-ta'akhkhur)
الانتزاع (al-intizāʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all relations are either entirely real or purely imaginary.
Correction: This classification shows that some relations have a real basis (exist externally) while others are mental abstractions from real things.

Checkpoint
According to the classification, which kind of relation is said to terminate at a limit?
A) Those invented by the mind
B) Those existing externally
C) Both kinds
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Relations are divided into those that exist externally and terminate at a limit, and those invented by the mind, which are unlimited.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage reveals that some relations we experience are not externally real but invented by our minds when we compare things. What is one relation in your daily life—perhaps a judgment about your status or worth—that might be a mental invention? How would recognizing it as mental change the weight you give it?
?
Al-Iji states that external relations terminate at a limit, but mental relations have no limit. Consider your desires: are they based on external relations that should be limited, or have they become unlimited mental inventions? What is one desire you need to bring back to its proper limit?

The Verbal Dispute Over the Nature of al-Kaun
Distinguishing two definitions of rest and their consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the verbal dispute about whether the first occurrence (al-kaun) is rest or not, and how different definitions of rest affect the composition of motion.

Core Idea
Whether motion is composed of rests depends on how you define 'rest'.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif addresses a key debate in kalam: Is the first occurrence of a substance in a place considered rest? The discussion hinges on a verbal distinction. Depending on the definition of rest, the first occurrence may or may not be part of motion. Let's examine the two definitions and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
The author states that the first occurrence is considered rest and is not part of motion. Note the phrase 'it is apparent'.
2
Identify the two definitions of rest
The passage presents two interpretations: rest as absolute obtaining in a place, and rest as an occurrence preceded by another occurrence in the same place. Compare the consequences.
3
Analyze the implications for motion
Under the first definition, motion is composed of rests; under the second, the first occurrence is an intermediate and motion is not composed of rests.
4
Reflect on the verbal nature of the dispute
The author calls the dispute 'verbal' because it depends on definition, not on a real disagreement about the nature of the occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجب أن يكون جزءاً للحركة، وهو ظاهر فإن الكون في أول الحدوث سكون عندهم وليس جزءاً لحركة أصلاً ) والنزاع) في أن الكون في أول زمان الحدوث سكون أو ليس بسكون (لفظي ) فإنه إن فسر السكون بالحصول في المكان مطلقاً كان ذلك الكون سكوناً، ولزم تركب الحركة من السكنات لأنها مركبة من الأكوان الأول في الأحياز كما عرفت، وإن فسر بالكون المسبوق بكون آخر في ذلك الحيز لم يكن ذلك الكون سكوناً ولا حركة، بل واسطة بينهما ولم يلزم أيضاً تركب الحركة من السكنات، فإن الكون الأول في المكان الثاني أعني الدخول فيه هو عين الخروج من المكان الأول

وبأن كون الثاني سكوناً يستلزم كون الأول ، فلما لم يقولوا بكون السكون الثاني حركة بأن يعتبروا فيه عدم المسبوقية بالحصول في ذلك الحيز مع أنه مثل الكون الأول الثاني هو حركة بالاتفاق فقد اعترفوا ببطلان ذلك المعنى، إما ببطلان التماثل كما نقله من الآمدي، أو لعدم وجود الاشتراك في المتماثلين فلا يلزمهم على هذا أن تكون الحركة مجموع سكنات مع اعترافهم بهذا اللزوم، وبالجملة خلاصة الإشكال الذي أورده أن الجواب الثاني لا يتأتى من جانبهم لمنافاته لقولهم: بتركب الحركة من السكنات وهذا كلام حق لايرد عليه قوله : وهو مردود انتهى. ولا يخفى اندفاعه بما حررناه سابقاً من أن تماثل الكون الأول بالثاني من حيث إيجابه الاختصاص بالحيز الثاني لا يستلزم تماثله من حيث إيجابه الخروج من الحيز الأول حتى يلزم أن لا يكون الحركة مركبة من السكنات .

قوله : ( وليس جزءاً لحركة أصلاً) أي إذا بقي ذلك الجوهر في ذلك المكان أو العدم في الآن الثاني بخلاف ما إذا انتقل منه الآن إلى جزء آخر فإن مجموع الكونين حركة عند من يقول بتركب الحركة من السكنات .

المصنف، وحينئذ لا تكون الحركة مجموع سكنات أن الكون الثاني سكون وليس جزءاً للحركة كما زعمه والظاهر أن معنى قوله وحينئذ لا تكون الحركة مجموع سكنات، أن لزوم كون : الحركة مجموع السكنات والتزامهم إياه كان مبنياً على لزوم ذلك لقولهم بتماثل الحصولين وبأن كون الثاني سكوناً يستلزم كون الأول كذلك على ما تحققت فلما لم يقولوا بكون السكون الثاني حركة بأن اعتبروا فيها عدم المسبوقية فيه بالحصول في ذلك الحيز مع أنه مثل الكون الأول وهو حركة بالاتفاق اعترفوا ببطلان ذلك إما ببطلان التماثل كما نقله مع الآمدي أو بعدم وجوب اشتراك التماثلين فلا يلزمهم على هذا أن تكون الحركة مجموع السكنات مع اعترافهم بهذا اللزوم وبالجملة خلاصة الإشكال الذي أورده أن الجواب المذكور لاينافي من جانبهم وهذا كلام عليه قوله : وهو مردود إلخ غاية ما في الباب أن يكون في العبارة أدنى مسامحة فتدبر. قوله: (وليس جزءاً لحركة أصلاً) الظاهر أنّ هذا إنما هو إذا انعدم الكائن في الآن الثاني، وإلا فإذا حدث جوهر في حيز ثم انتقل منه إلى حيز يليه وانعدم فيه، فالحركة مجموع الحصولين عند من يقول بتركب الحركة من الأكوان فافهم.
It must be a part of motion, and it is apparent that the occurrence (al-kaun) at the first moment of origination is rest (sukūn) according to them, and it is not a part of motion at all. And the dispute is about whether the occurrence at the first moment of time is rest or not rest (verbal). For if rest is interpreted as obtaining in place absolutely, then that occurrence is rest, and the composition of motion from rests follows necessarily because it is composed of the first occurrences in the places as you know. And if it is interpreted as the occurrence preceded by another occurrence in that place, then that occurrence is neither rest nor motion, but an intermediate between them, and the composition of motion from rests does not necessarily follow. For the first occurrence in the second place, i.e., entering into it, is identical to exiting from the first place.

And because the second occurrence being rest necessitates that the first be rest, but when they did not say that the second rest is motion by considering in it the lack of being preceded by obtaining in that place, although it is like the first occurrence which is motion by agreement, they thus acknowledged the invalidity of that meaning, either due to the invalidity of similarity as reported from al-Amidi, or due to the lack of commonality in similar things. So according to this, it does not necessarily follow for them that motion is the sum of rests, despite their acknowledgment of this consequence. In summary, the essence of the objection raised is that the second answer cannot be achieved from their side because it contradicts their statement that motion is composed of rests. This is a correct statement and is not refuted by his saying: 'It is refuted' end. And it is not hidden that it is repelled by what we have previously established: that the similarity of the first occurrence to the second in terms of necessitating specification to the second place does not necessitate their similarity in terms of necessitating exit from the first place, such that it would necessarily follow that motion is not composed of rests.

His saying: '(and it is not a part of motion at all)' meaning if that substance remains in that place or ceases to exist in the second moment, unlike if it moves from it to another part, then the sum of the two occurrences is motion according to those who say motion is composed of rests.

The author: Then motion would not be the sum of rests, that the second occurrence is rest and not a part of motion as he claimed. And it appears that the meaning of his saying 'then motion would not be the sum of rests' is that the necessity of motion being the sum of rests and their commitment to it was based on the necessity of that due to their saying that the two obtainings are similar and that the second being rest necessitates that the first be likewise, as you have verified. So when they did not say that the second rest is motion by considering in it the lack of being preceded by obtaining in that place, although it is like the first occurrence which is motion by agreement, they acknowledged the invalidity of that, either due to the invalidity of similarity as transmitted with al-Amidi, or due to the non-necessity of commonality in similar things. So according to this, it does not necessarily follow for them that motion is the sum of rests, despite their acknowledgment of this consequence. In summary, the essence of the objection raised is that the mentioned answer does not contradict their side. This statement is subject to his saying: 'It is refuted etc.' The utmost is that there is a slight looseness in the expression, so ponder. His saying: '(and it is not a part of motion at all)' apparently applies only if the existing thing ceases in the second moment. Otherwise, if a substance occurs in a place and then moves from it to an adjacent place and ceases to exist in it, then motion is the sum of the two obtainings according to those who say motion is composed of occurrences, so understand.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the nature of al-kaun (the occurrence) and whether motion is composed of rests (sakanat). The author begins by stating that the occurrence at the first moment of origination is considered rest (sukūn) by the theologians and is not a part of motion. Then the dispute is clarified as verbal: if rest is defined absolutely as obtaining in a place, then that first occurrence is rest, and it necessarily follows that motion is composed of rests. If rest is defined as an occurrence preceded by another occurrence in that same place, then the first occurrence is neither rest nor motion but an intermediate, and motion is not composed of rests. The author then addresses an objection: if the second occurrence is rest, then the first must also be rest, but theologians disagree; thus they admit the invalidity of that reasoning. The objection is answered by distinguishing between two aspects of similarity: similarity in necessitating specification to the second place does not imply similarity in necessitating exit from the first place, so motion can still be composed of rests. The commentary further clarifies the conditions under which motion is composed of rests, defending the Ash'ari doctrine of composition of motion from rests.

Key Terms
كون
سكون
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Ash'ari position necessarily makes motion a sum of rests, but the passage shows it depends on definition.
Correction: The passage clarifies that motion being composed of rests is a consequence of a particular definition of rest; with a different definition, motion is not composed of rests.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of interpreting rest as obtaining in a place absolutely?
A) The first occurrence is neither rest nor motion
B) Motion is composed of rests
C) The first occurrence is motion
D) Motion is not composed of rests

Key Takeaway
The verbal dispute about whether motion is composed of rests is resolved by clarifying the definition of rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that a single term—'rest'—can be defined in two ways, leading to opposite conclusions about motion. In your own life, have you ever held a firm opinion on a religious matter only to realize later that the disagreement was merely verbal? Can you think of one such issue right now? If so, what does that teach you about humility and the pursuit of truth?
?
The passage shows that the first occurrence in a place is identical to exiting the previous place. This unity of entering and exiting is paradoxical. Reflect: in your spiritual journey, are there moments where leaving a bad habit is identical to embracing a good one? That is, can you think of a single act that is both an exit from sin and an entry into obedience? Describe it honestly to yourself now and commit to that act.

Objection and Answer on the Composition of Motion
Objection and answer regarding similarity and composition of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if the second occurrence is rest then the first must also be rest, and the answer using the concept of similarity.

Core Idea
Two events can be similar in one aspect but different in another, avoiding the need for motion to be composed of rests.

After establishing the verbal dispute, the text presents an objection: if the second occurrence in a place is considered rest, then by similarity the first occurrence should also be rest, leading to a contradiction. The answer distinguishes between two kinds of similarity. Let's trace the argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector argues that if the second occurrence is rest, then the first must be rest too because they are similar. But theologians do not consider the second rest as motion, so they admit the invalidity of the similarity argument.
2
Examine the two possible reasons for invalidity
The passage mentions two reasons: either similarity itself is invalid (as per al-Amidi) or the two events do not share all relevant attributes.
3
See the answer from the author
The author responds that similarity in one respect (necessitating specification to a place) does not imply similarity in another respect (necessitating exit from a place), so the objection fails.
4
Understand how motion is composed of rests
The passage concludes that motion can still be composed of rests because the first occurrence is not similar to the second in the relevant way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجب أن يكون جزءاً للحركة، وهو ظاهر فإن الكون في أول الحدوث سكون عندهم وليس جزءاً لحركة أصلاً ) والنزاع) في أن الكون في أول زمان الحدوث سكون أو ليس بسكون (لفظي ) فإنه إن فسر السكون بالحصول في المكان مطلقاً كان ذلك الكون سكوناً، ولزم تركب الحركة من السكنات لأنها مركبة من الأكوان الأول في الأحياز كما عرفت، وإن فسر بالكون المسبوق بكون آخر في ذلك الحيز لم يكن ذلك الكون سكوناً ولا حركة، بل واسطة بينهما ولم يلزم أيضاً تركب الحركة من السكنات، فإن الكون الأول في المكان الثاني أعني الدخول فيه هو عين الخروج من المكان الأول

وبأن كون الثاني سكوناً يستلزم كون الأول ، فلما لم يقولوا بكون السكون الثاني حركة بأن يعتبروا فيه عدم المسبوقية بالحصول في ذلك الحيز مع أنه مثل الكون الأول الثاني هو حركة بالاتفاق فقد اعترفوا ببطلان ذلك المعنى، إما ببطلان التماثل كما نقله من الآمدي، أو لعدم وجود الاشتراك في المتماثلين فلا يلزمهم على هذا أن تكون الحركة مجموع سكنات مع اعترافهم بهذا اللزوم، وبالجملة خلاصة الإشكال الذي أورده أن الجواب الثاني لا يتأتى من جانبهم لمنافاته لقولهم: بتركب الحركة من السكنات وهذا كلام حق لايرد عليه قوله : وهو مردود انتهى. ولا يخفى اندفاعه بما حررناه سابقاً من أن تماثل الكون الأول بالثاني من حيث إيجابه الاختصاص بالحيز الثاني لا يستلزم تماثله من حيث إيجابه الخروج من الحيز الأول حتى يلزم أن لا يكون الحركة مركبة من السكنات .

قوله : ( وليس جزءاً لحركة أصلاً) أي إذا بقي ذلك الجوهر في ذلك المكان أو العدم في الآن الثاني بخلاف ما إذا انتقل منه الآن إلى جزء آخر فإن مجموع الكونين حركة عند من يقول بتركب الحركة من السكنات .

المصنف، وحينئذ لا تكون الحركة مجموع سكنات أن الكون الثاني سكون وليس جزءاً للحركة كما زعمه والظاهر أن معنى قوله وحينئذ لا تكون الحركة مجموع سكنات، أن لزوم كون : الحركة مجموع السكنات والتزامهم إياه كان مبنياً على لزوم ذلك لقولهم بتماثل الحصولين وبأن كون الثاني سكوناً يستلزم كون الأول كذلك على ما تحققت فلما لم يقولوا بكون السكون الثاني حركة بأن اعتبروا فيها عدم المسبوقية فيه بالحصول في ذلك الحيز مع أنه مثل الكون الأول وهو حركة بالاتفاق اعترفوا ببطلان ذلك إما ببطلان التماثل كما نقله مع الآمدي أو بعدم وجوب اشتراك التماثلين فلا يلزمهم على هذا أن تكون الحركة مجموع السكنات مع اعترافهم بهذا اللزوم وبالجملة خلاصة الإشكال الذي أورده أن الجواب المذكور لاينافي من جانبهم وهذا كلام عليه قوله : وهو مردود إلخ غاية ما في الباب أن يكون في العبارة أدنى مسامحة فتدبر. قوله: (وليس جزءاً لحركة أصلاً) الظاهر أنّ هذا إنما هو إذا انعدم الكائن في الآن الثاني، وإلا فإذا حدث جوهر في حيز ثم انتقل منه إلى حيز يليه وانعدم فيه، فالحركة مجموع الحصولين عند من يقول بتركب الحركة من الأكوان فافهم.
It must be a part of motion, and it is apparent that the occurrence (al-kaun) at the first moment of origination is rest (sukūn) according to them, and it is not a part of motion at all. And the dispute is about whether the occurrence at the first moment of time is rest or not rest (verbal). For if rest is interpreted as obtaining in place absolutely, then that occurrence is rest, and the composition of motion from rests follows necessarily because it is composed of the first occurrences in the places as you know. And if it is interpreted as the occurrence preceded by another occurrence in that place, then that occurrence is neither rest nor motion, but an intermediate between them, and the composition of motion from rests does not necessarily follow. For the first occurrence in the second place, i.e., entering into it, is identical to exiting from the first place.

And because the second occurrence being rest necessitates that the first be rest, but when they did not say that the second rest is motion by considering in it the lack of being preceded by obtaining in that place, although it is like the first occurrence which is motion by agreement, they thus acknowledged the invalidity of that meaning, either due to the invalidity of similarity as reported from al-Amidi, or due to the lack of commonality in similar things. So according to this, it does not necessarily follow for them that motion is the sum of rests, despite their acknowledgment of this consequence. In summary, the essence of the objection raised is that the second answer cannot be achieved from their side because it contradicts their statement that motion is composed of rests. This is a correct statement and is not refuted by his saying: 'It is refuted' end. And it is not hidden that it is repelled by what we have previously established: that the similarity of the first occurrence to the second in terms of necessitating specification to the second place does not necessitate their similarity in terms of necessitating exit from the first place, such that it would necessarily follow that motion is not composed of rests.

His saying: '(and it is not a part of motion at all)' meaning if that substance remains in that place or ceases to exist in the second moment, unlike if it moves from it to another part, then the sum of the two occurrences is motion according to those who say motion is composed of rests.

The author: Then motion would not be the sum of rests, that the second occurrence is rest and not a part of motion as he claimed. And it appears that the meaning of his saying 'then motion would not be the sum of rests' is that the necessity of motion being the sum of rests and their commitment to it was based on the necessity of that due to their saying that the two obtainings are similar and that the second being rest necessitates that the first be likewise, as you have verified. So when they did not say that the second rest is motion by considering in it the lack of being preceded by obtaining in that place, although it is like the first occurrence which is motion by agreement, they acknowledged the invalidity of that, either due to the invalidity of similarity as transmitted with al-Amidi, or due to the non-necessity of commonality in similar things. So according to this, it does not necessarily follow for them that motion is the sum of rests, despite their acknowledgment of this consequence. In summary, the essence of the objection raised is that the mentioned answer does not contradict their side. This statement is subject to his saying: 'It is refuted etc.' The utmost is that there is a slight looseness in the expression, so ponder. His saying: '(and it is not a part of motion at all)' apparently applies only if the existing thing ceases in the second moment. Otherwise, if a substance occurs in a place and then moves from it to an adjacent place and ceases to exist in it, then motion is the sum of the two obtainings according to those who say motion is composed of occurrences, so understand.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the nature of al-kaun (the occurrence) and whether motion is composed of rests (sakanat). The author begins by stating that the occurrence at the first moment of origination is considered rest (sukūn) by the theologians and is not a part of motion. Then the dispute is clarified as verbal: if rest is defined absolutely as obtaining in a place, then that first occurrence is rest, and it necessarily follows that motion is composed of rests. If rest is defined as an occurrence preceded by another occurrence in that same place, then the first occurrence is neither rest nor motion but an intermediate, and motion is not composed of rests. The author then addresses an objection: if the second occurrence is rest, then the first must also be rest, but theologians disagree; thus they admit the invalidity of that reasoning. The objection is answered by distinguishing between two aspects of similarity: similarity in necessitating specification to the second place does not imply similarity in necessitating exit from the first place, so motion can still be composed of rests. The commentary further clarifies the conditions under which motion is composed of rests, defending the Ash'ari doctrine of composition of motion from rests.

Key Terms
كون
سكون
حركة
سكنات
تماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Ash'ari doctrine of motion composed of rests leads to a logical contradiction as shown by the objection.
Correction: The passage shows that the objection is not valid because the two occurrences are not fully similar; the first involves exit, so motion can still be composed of rests.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the objection about similarity fail?
A) Because similarity is always invalid
B) Because the two occurrences are not similar in all respects
C) Because the second occurrence is motion
D) Because the first occurrence is not similar to the second

Key Takeaway
The objection that motion cannot be composed of rests because of similarity is answered by showing the two occurrences differ in a key respect: the first involves exit, the second does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical objection to a belief you hold dear, do you immediately assume the objection must be correct because it appears sound? This lesson shows that an objection can be refuted by uncovering hidden differences. Reflect on a time when you accepted a criticism without examining all aspects. How will you change your approach going forward?
?
Are you willing to put in the intellectual effort to defend sound beliefs, even when the opposition seems strong? Or do you let intellectual laziness cause you to abandon truths? Commit to studying objections thoroughly, distinguishing where similarity holds and where it breaks.
?
The objection in this passage falsely assumed full similarity. In your own life, have you ever judged someone else's actions or words as hypocritical because you assumed similarity between two situations that actually differed in key respects? How can you train yourself to look for hidden distinctions before condemning?

Social Contact and Separation in Kalam Atomism
Defines social contact and separation based on interposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam definitions of social contact and separation based on interposition, and why social contact is not a single accident shared by two substances.

Core Idea
Social contact is defined by the impossibility of a third substance interposing between two.

In kalam atomistic physics, the relationship between two substances is defined by whether a third substance can fit between them. This deceptively simple criterion leads to profound discussions about the nature of accidents and how they inhere in substances. The passage below from Sanusi's commentary establishes the foundational definitions and clarifies a key misconception.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Focus on the criterion: if a third can interpose, it's separation; otherwise social contact.
Notice that the author emphasizes possibility, not actual interposition.
2
Identify the asymmetry between social contact and separation
Social contact is one type, while separation has many varieties: proximity, distance, adjacency. Note that adjacency will later be reinterpreted as social contact.
This shows that the classification is not symmetrical.
3
Understand why social contact is not a single accident
The passage argues that social contact cannot be a single accident inhering in both substances. Instead, each substance has its own 'social contact' accident, identical in essence but different in identity.
This avoids the problem of one accident in two loci.
4
Reflect on the importance of this definition
The author warns that this point escapes many great masters. Understanding this distinction prevents philosophical errors about accidents and inherence.
This is a foundational concept in kalam ontology.

Source Text

ولا شك أن الخروج عن الأول حركة فكذا الدخول فيه ( وأما الأول ) وهو حصول الجوهر في الجوهر في الحيز بالنسبة إلى جوهر آخر ( فإن كان بحيث يمكن أن يتخلل بينه وبين ذلك (الآخر) جوهر ( ثالث فهو الافتراق وإلا فهو الاجتماع وإنما قلنا : إمكان التخلل دون وقوع التخلل لجواز أن يكون بينهما خلاء) أي مكان خال عن المتحيز ( عند المتكلمين) فإنهم يجوزونه ) فالاجتماع واحد ) لا يتصور إلا على وجه واحد هو أن لا يمكن تخلل ثالث بينهما ) والافتراق مختلف) على وجوه متنوعة ( فمنه قرب و ) منه ( بعد متفاوت) في مراتب البعد (و) منه (مجاورة ) جعلها من أقسام الافتراق، وسيصرح بأن المجاورة عين الاجتماع ) واعلم أن الاجتماع قائم بكل جزء ) أي جوهر ( بالنسبة إلى الآخر لا أنه أمر ) واحد (قائم بهما ) معافاته غير جائز أن العرض الواحد لا يقوم بشيئين لا على أن يقوم بكل واحد منهما، وهو ظاهر ولا أن يقوم بهما معاً وإلا لم يكن واحداً حقيقة ( أو وضع أحدهما ) أي ولا أن الاجتماع وضع أحد الجوهرين بالنسبة إلى الآخر فإنهم ) أي المتكلمين ( لا يثبتونه ) أي الوضع ويثبتون الاجتماع ( فالجوهران ) المجتمعان (كل) منهما له اجتماع بالآخر قائم به فهناك اجتماعان متحدان بالماهية ومختلفان بالهوية ( فاحفظ هذا الذي ذكرناه ) فإنه مما يذهب على كثير من عظماء الصناعة ( الكلامية

قوله : ( لا يتصور إلخ) ليس المراد أنه واحد شخصي وهو ظن ولا أنه واحد نوعي لما يجيء أنّ الأكوان كلها نوع واحد .
قوله : ( في مراتب (البعد) خص البعد بالتفاوت رعاية لقرب الموصوف وإشارة إلى أن التفاوت في البعد عين التفاوت في القرب .
قوله : (جعلها من إلخ) هذه المجاورة يمكن بتخلل الجوهر الفرد فلا يكون اجتماعاً وفي تفسير المجاورة فيما سيأتي في المقصد الخامس بقوله : أي الاجتماع إشارة إلى أن هذا غير ما سبق .
قوله : ( وإلا لم يكن واحداً (حقيقة لما تقرر عندهم من أن انقسام المحل يستلزم انقسام الحال بناء على نفي الحلول الطرياتي كما مر .
قوله : ( متفاوت في مراتب البعد يجوز تعلق التفاوت بالقرب أيضاً وإن خصصه الشارح بالبعد القريب لإفراد العبارة وإن جاز توجيهه بإرادة كل منهما .
قوله : ( وسيصرح أن المجاورة عين الاجتماع قد يقال : المجاورة التي ذكرت بعد أريد بها معنى آخر غير الذي أريد بها هاهنا ولذا فسرها بقوله : أي الاجتماع على أن المجاورة بمعنى الاجتماع منقول من كلام الشيخ الأشعري والمعتزلة .
قوله : ( وإلا لم يكن واحداً (حقيقة قد سبق في بحث الحياة دفعه فلذا لم يتعرض له .
That it is considered—and there is no doubt that exiting from the first is motion, so too entering into it. (As for the first), which is the occurrence of a substance in a place relative to another substance, if it is such that a third substance can interpose between it and that other, then it is separation (iftiraq), otherwise it is social contact (ijtima'). And we only said possibility of interposition, not actual interposition, because there may be a void (khalā') between them, i.e., a place empty of extended substance, according to the theologians, for they allow it. So social contact is one, not conceivable except in one way: that a third cannot interpose between them. And separation is various, in diverse modes. Among them is proximity (qurb) and among them is distance (bu'd) varying in degrees of distance, and among them is adjacency (mujāwara). He made it among the divisions of separation, and he will state explicitly that adjacency is identical to social contact. Know that social contact subsists in each part, i.e., substance, in relation to the other, not that it is a single thing subsisting in both, because it is not permissible for a single accident to subsist in two things, neither by subsisting in each one of them—this is apparent—nor by subsisting in both together, for then it would not be truly one. Nor is it the position (waḍ') of one of them, i.e., nor is social contact the position of one substance in relation to the other, for the theologians do not affirm position and they affirm social contact. So the two substances, each one has a social contact with the other subsisting in it, so there are two social contacts identical in essence (māhiyya) and different in identity (huwiyya). So preserve what we have mentioned, for it escapes many of the great masters of the theological art.

His saying: '(not conceivable etc.)' The intent is not that it is one individual, that is an assumption, nor that it is one specific type, because it will come that all occurrences (akwān) are of one type. His saying: '(in degrees of distance)' He specifically mentioned distance with variation in consideration of the proximity of the described and as an indication that variation in distance is exactly variation in proximity. His saying: '(He made it among etc.)' This adjacency can have the individual substance interpose, so it is not social contact, and in the interpretation of adjacency later in the fifth chapter by his saying: 'i.e., social contact,' there is an indication that this is different from what preceded. His saying: '(otherwise it would not be truly one)' because it is established with them that division of the locus necessitates division of the accident inhering in it, based on the denial of accidental inherence (ḥulūl ṭabīʿī) as has passed. His saying: '(varying in degrees of distance)' It is permissible for variation to attach to proximity as well, even though the commentator specified it with near distance for the sake of brevity of expression, though it is possible to direct it intending each of them. His saying: '(and he will state explicitly that adjacency is identical to social contact)' It may be said that the adjacency mentioned later is intended with a different meaning than what is intended here, and therefore he interpreted it with his saying: 'i.e., social contact,' that adjacency in the sense of social contact is transmitted from the speech of Shaykh al-Ashʿarī and the Muʿtazila. His saying: '(otherwise it would not be truly one)' has already been repelled in the discussion of life, so he did not address it.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage defines social contact (ijtima') and separation (iftiraq) in the context of atomistic physics. The criterion is the possibility of interposition of a third substance between two substances: if no third substance can interpose, it is social contact; if a third can interpose, it is separation. Social contact is only one type, while separation has various degrees: proximity, distance, and adjacency. The author emphasizes that social contact is not a single accident shared between two substances, because an accident cannot inhere in two loci. Instead, each substance has its own social contact relation to the other, identical in essence (māhiyya) but distinct in identity (huwiyya). The commentary clarifies that social contact is not one individual nor one type, and that adjacency may be interpreted differently later. It also explains why social contact cannot be a position (waḍ').

هوية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Social contact is a single accident that subsists in both substances simultaneously.
Correction: Social contact is actually two accidents: each substance has its own social contact relation to the other, identical in essence but different in identity. A single accident cannot subsist in two loci.

What specific step will you take this week to ensure your personal social contact with Allah is real and not just proximity (qurb) or adjacency (mujāwara) that you mistake for intimacy?

Conjunction is One, Separation is Diverse
Al-Jurjani states that ijtima' is single, while iftiraq has multiple varieties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conjunction (ijtima') is considered a single type while separation (iftiraq) has multiple varieties.

Core Idea
Conjunction has one definition; separation has many forms.

In this brief passage, al-Jurjani clarifies a fundamental distinction in how substances relate to one another. While conjunction is a simple, one-way relation, separation can take many forms. This sets the stage for understanding how theologians talk about bodies and their positions. Let's unpack the classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: ijtima', iftiraq, qurb, bu'd, mujawara.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the single nature of ijtima'
The text says ijtima' is one because it has only one condition: no third substance can be between the two. That is its complete definition.
Contrast this with iftiraq.
3
Identify the types of iftiraq
The passage lists three types: qurb (proximity), bu'd (distance), and mujawara (adjacency). Note that bu'd has degrees, but qurb might also have degrees; the author chooses one for convenience.
4
Resolve the puzzle about mujawara
Mujawara is initially listed as a type of iftiraq, but the text hints that it will later be identified with ijtima'. This anticipates a clarification: adjacency is actually conjunction, not separation.
This is the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالاجتماع واحد لا يتصور إلا على وجه واحد هو أن لا يمكن تخلل ثالث بينهما والافتراق مختلف على وجوه متنوعة فمنه قرب ومنه بعد متفاوت في مراتب البعد ومنه مجاورة جعلها من أقسام الافتراق، وسيصرح بأن المجاورة عين الاجتماع
Thus conjunction (ijtima') is one; it cannot be conceived except in one way, namely that a third cannot be interposed between them. Separation (iftiraq) is diverse, having various types: among them is proximity (qurb), among them is distance (bu'd) varying in degrees of remoteness, and among them is adjacency (mujawara) – he made it a type of separation, but he will explicitly state that adjacency is identical with conjunction.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage classifies the two relations: ijtima' and iftiraq. Ijtima' is said to be one (single type) because it has only one condition: no possible interposition by a third substance. In contrast, iftiraq has multiple varieties: proximity (qurb), distance (bu'd) with varying degrees, and adjacency (mujawara). The author notes that adjacency is initially listed as a type of separation, but a later passage will clarify that adjacency is actually the same as conjunction. The commentary explains that 'distance' is specified as having degrees, but proximity also has degrees; the choice of wording is for convenience. The point is that separation admits variation while conjunction does not.

Key Terms
قرب (qurb)
بعد (bu'd)
مجاورة (mujawara)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that adjacency (mujawara) is a genuine form of separation like distance.
Correction: The text anticipates that adjacency will be identified with conjunction, not separation. So while it is listed as a type of iftiraq, it is really ijtima'.

Checkpoint
Why is conjunction (ijtima') considered one type?
A) Because it can be conceived in multiple ways
B) Because it always involves two substances
C) Because it has only one definition: impossibility of interposition
D) Because it includes proximity and distance

Key Takeaway
Conjunction is a single, uniform relation; separation is diverse, with multiple types including proximity, distance, and adjacency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Conjunction is defined as 'no third can interpose.' In your relationship with Allah, is there anything—a habit, a distraction, a sin—that interposes between you and Him? What is one concrete step you can take today to remove it?
?
The passage says separation has many degrees, from proximity to distance. Reflect on your own spiritual state: are you in proximity to acts of worship but distant from sincerity? Identify one act you do where your heart is far, and promise to bring it closer starting now.
?
Adjacency is eventually revealed to be conjunction. Are there aspects of your life you treat as separate from your faith—like work, study, or relationships—that should actually be part of your devotion? What would change if you saw them as part of your worship?

The Ontological Status of Conjunction in Each Atom
Al-Jurjani explains that conjunction is an accident in each atom, not a single accident subsisting in both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conjunction in kalam is considered two accidents (one in each atom) rather than a single relational accident.

Core Idea
Two conjunctions, one in each atom: identical in essence, distinct in identity.

Al-Jurjani addresses a subtle but crucial point in kalam ontology: the nature of conjunction between atoms. Many scholars mistakenly think it is a single accident linking two atoms. The author clarifies that it must be two accidents, each inhering in one atom, because an accident cannot subsist in two subjects. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the author's emphasis at the end.
2
Understand the core claim
Identify that the author states conjunction subsists in each atom, not as a single thing in both. This is the main thesis.
Contrast with the common view.
3
Examine the justification
Follow the reasoning why a single accident cannot subsist in two subjects. Consider the two possibilities he refutes.
This is a key logical move.
4
Grasp the conclusion and its importance
Realize that there are two conjunctions identical in quiddity but distinct in identity. Appreciate the warning that many scholars erred here.
This distinguishes the kalam view from other philosophical positions.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الاجتماع قائم بكل جزء أي جوهر بالنسبة إلى الآخر لا أنه أمر واحد قائم بهما معا فإنه غير جائز أن العرض الواحد لا يقوم بشيئين لا على أن يقوم بكل واحد منهما، وهو ظاهر ولا أن يقوم بهما معاً وإلا لم يكن واحداً حقيقة أو وضع أحدهما أي ولا أن الاجتماع وضع أحد الجوهرين بالنسبة إلى الآخر فإنهم أي المتكلمين لا يثبتونه أي الوضع ويثبتون الاجتماع فالجوهران المجتمعان كل منهما له اجتماع بالآخر قائم به فهناك اجتماعان متحدان بالماهية ومختلفان بالهوية فاحفظ هذا الذي ذكرناه فإنه مما يذهب على كثير من عظماء الصناعة الكلامية
Know that conjunction (ijtima') subsists in each part, i.e., each substance, in relation to the other; it is not a single thing subsisting in both. It is not possible for a single accident to subsist in two subjects – not in the sense that it subsists in each of them individually, that is obvious, nor that it subsists in both together, for then it would not be truly one. Nor is it the position (wad') of one of them relative to the other, for the theologians do not affirm position but affirm conjunction. Thus two conjoined substances each have a conjunction with the other subsisting in it; so there are two conjunctions identical in quiddity but distinct in identity. So preserve what we have mentioned, for many great masters of the theological craft have erred on it.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ontological nature of conjunction. It is not a single accident inhering in both atoms simultaneously; rather, each atom has its own conjunction accident relative to the other. This is because an accident cannot subsist in two subjects – neither by being individually in each (that would make two accidents) nor by being common to both (that would not be a single accident). Furthermore, conjunction is not a relation of 'position' (wad') of one atom relative to the other, because the mutakallimun do not accept position as a real category; they affirm conjunction instead. Therefore, in a pair of conjoined atoms, there are two conjunctions: one in each atom. They are identical in essence (mahiyya) but different in individual identity (huwiyya). The author emphasizes that this subtle point has eluded many great scholars of kalam.

Key Terms
عرض واحد
وضع
ماهية
هوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conjunction is a single relational accident subsisting between two atoms.
Correction: Conjunction consists of two accidents, one in each atom, because a single accident cannot subsist in two subjects.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how many conjunctions exist between two conjoined atoms?
A) One, shared by both
B) Two, one in each atom
C) None, it is a relation of position
D) Two, but they are both identical in identity

Key Takeaway
Conjunction between two atoms is not a single accident but two accidents, one in each atom, identical in essence and distinct in identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a close relationship in your life (with a spouse, parent, or friend). Have you ever assumed that the bond between you is a single 'thing' shared equally? How might acknowledging that each of you has your own distinct 'conjunction'—your own feelings, responsibilities, and perspective—change the way you nurture that relationship? Today, will you take one concrete step to honor the other's distinct reality?
?
Al-Jurjani warns that even great masters of theology have erred on this subtle point. When was the last time you discovered a significant error in a firmly held belief about your faith? Are you actively seeking correction even when it is uncomfortable? What will you do this week to examine one of your own deeply held assumptions with the same rigor he applied?

Philosophers on Rest and the Metaphorical Nature of Kawn Types
Al-Jurjani presents the philosophers' definition of rest and clarifies that the four kawn types are metaphorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers define rest as a privation and why the four types of kawn are considered metaphorical rather than real distinct types.

Core Idea
Rest is absence of motion; the four kawn 'types' are metaphorical labels for one reality.

This passage completes the discussion of the four types of kawn (conjunction, separation, motion, rest) by addressing two important clarifications. First, the philosophers define rest not as a positive state but as the absence of motion, which has implications for immaterial beings. Second, the author warns that calling these four 'types' is merely metaphorical—in reality there is only one type of kawn with varying relations. These points sharpen your understanding of what kawn really is and how it relates to the objects it describes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify its two main parts
First, read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Notice that the passage has two clear sections: the philosophers' definition of rest (up to 'لَيْسَ مِنْ شَأْنِهَا الْحَرَكَةُ') and the caution about the types of kawn being metaphorical (from 'تَنْبِيهٌ' onwards).
The passage ends mid-sentence; the continuation is not included.
2
Analyze the definition of rest and its consequence for immaterial entities
The philosophers define rest as 'absence of motion from that whose nature is to be movable'. Since immaterial entities (mujarradat) are not subject to motion, they cannot be described as resting. Understand that this makes rest a privative concept.
This contrasts with the mutakallimun who consider rest a positive state (a kawn).
3
Examine the caution about metaphorical types
The author argues that the distinguishing features of kawn are merely conceptual, not real differentiae. Therefore, calling them 'types' is metaphorical; in reality there is one kawn with varying attributes. The example of a single substance with no conjunction or separation illustrates this point.
Even the same kawn can be both conjunction and separation depending on perspective.
4
Reflect on the implications for individual identity
Consider why attributes that do not change quiddity may not even change individual identity. The example shows that kawn is a single reality that takes on different relations without becoming a different type.
This supports the Ash'ari position that kawn is one attribute with diverse states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الحكماء : السكون عدم الحركة عما من شأنه أن يكون متحركاً فالمجردات لا توصف بالسكون الذي هو أمر عدمي عندهم إذ ليس من شأنها الحركة. تنبيه إذا قلنا : ليس في الخارج إلا الكون والفصول المميزة المذكورة أمور اعتبارية لا فصول حقيقية منوعة كان تسميتها أنواعاً مجازاً وإنما هو نوع واحد له صفات متخالفة، لا توجب اختلافاً في الماهية بل ربما لا توجب أيضاً اختلافا في الهوية الشخصية إذا الكون الواحد بالشخص يعرض له أنه اجتماع بالنسبة إلى جزء وافتراق بالنسبة إلى جزء آخر ولو فرضنا جوهراً فرداً خلقه الله تعالى وحده لم يتصف باجتماع ولا افتراق ما دام منفردا وإذا خلق الله تعالى بعد ذلك يعرض
The philosophers (al-hukama') said: Rest (sukun) is the absence of motion from that whose nature is to be movable. Hence immaterial entities are not described with rest, which is a privative thing for them, since motion is not in their nature. A caution: If we say that in the external world there exists nothing but al-kawn, and the aforementioned distinguishing features are conceptual matters, not real differentiating differentiae, then calling them 'types' is metaphorical. Rather, it is one type with varying attributes that do not necessitate a difference in quiddity; indeed, perhaps they do not even necessitate a difference in individual identity. For the same individual kawn can be conjunction with respect to one part and separation with respect to another part. And if we suppose a single substance created by God alone, it would not be described by conjunction or separation as long as it remains alone. Then if God subsequently creates another [substance], it would acquire...
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage first presents the position of the philosophers (al-hukama') on rest (sukun): they define it as the absence of motion in that which is capable of motion. Thus immaterial beings (mujarradat) are not described as 'resting' because they are not subject to motion. The text then gives a 'caution' (tanbih) that follows from the earlier thesis that the distinguishing features of the four types of kawn are merely conceptual. If they are conceptual, then calling them 'types' is metaphorical; in reality, there is only one type of kawn with varying attributes. These attributes do not cause a difference in quiddity (mahiyya), and perhaps not even a difference in individual identity (huwiyya shakhsiyya). For example, the same kawn can be conjunction in relation to one part and separation in relation to another. If a single substance existed alone, it would have no conjunction or separation at all. Only when God creates another substance do these relations appear. The commentary clarifies that 'by testimony of sense' refers to intellectual judgment based on sense, not that kawn itself is directly sensible.

Key Terms
سكون (sukun)
عدم الحركة ('adam al-haraka)
مجردات (mujarradat)
مجاز (majaz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rest is a positive quality or state inherent in objects.
Correction: The philosophers define rest as the absence of motion (a privation) in objects capable of motion. It is not a positive attribute.

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers (al-hukama') say that immaterial entities (mujarradat) are not described as 'resting'?
A) Because they are always in motion
B) Because rest is the absence of motion, and they are not subject to motion
C) Because rest is a positive attribute they possess
D) Because they are not in space

Key Takeaway
The philosophers define rest as absence of motion, and the four 'types' of kawn are merely metaphorical labels for one underlying reality with varying relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience stillness in your life, do you see it as a positive state of peace, or as merely the absence of activity? How does the philosophers' definition of rest as privation challenge your own understanding of 'rest' in your relationship with Allah?
?
If the four types of kawn are merely conceptual labels for one reality, then your own 'states' of being—what you call 'resting,' 'moving,' 'alone,' 'together'—are not permanent realities. Are you clinging to a label (like 'busy' or 'at rest') as if it defines your essence? How would your life change if you saw these as temporary relational descriptions?
?
The philosophers say immaterial entities are not described with rest. Your soul is immaterial. Where will your soul find true rest—not just absence of motion, but something beyond? Do you treat your soul as if it can be satisfied by worldly stillness, or do you seek the rest that comes only from the Creator?

Al-Kawn and Its Four Types: One Reality with Accidental Distinctions
The author defines al-kawn and its four types, explaining they are one reality with accidental distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of al-kawn as a single reality with four modes distinguished only by mental considerations, and why the mutakallimun regard motion, rest, conjunction, and separation as accidental attributes rather than distinct species.

Core Idea
The four types of al-kawn are one reality with mental distinctions.

This lesson introduces the foundational kalam concept of al-kawn (occupying space). The mutakallimun recognized four phenomena—motion, rest, conjunction, separation—but they considered them mere modes of a single reality, distinguished by conceptual features like temporal precedence or possibility of an intervening third. Understanding this distinction prevents the error of reifying these modes into separate species.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and types
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the definition of al-kawn and the listing of its four types. Pay attention to the author's statement that their outcome returns to a single reality.
2
Identify the mental considerations
Find where the author explains that the distinguishing features (like being preceded by another kawn) are mental considerations (umūr iʿtibāriyya) and not real differentiating genera.
Contrast this with the philosophers' view that rest is absence of motion.
3
Reflect on the example of the atom
Consider the example of an individual atom: alone it has no conjunction/separation; when another atom appears, it acquires the attribute. This illustrates that the same kawn can be called different names relative to different parts.
This shows that the types are not fixed species but accidental relations.

The fourth observation - the third purpose: al-kawn and its four types. Among them is one who imagines that a single occupying (social?) is subsisting in two loci, and among them is one who imagines that the occupying is of the category of position. Likewise. [The third purpose: al-kawn and its four types] (al-kawn) meaning the occurrence in a space (its existence is necessary by the testimony of sense) and likewise its four types according to the mutakallimun exist (for their outcome, as you have learned, from the division returns to al-kawn) which is one type in reality (and the distinguishing features) by which those types are distinguished from each other (are mental considerations) not real differentiating genera (such as its being preceded by another kawn) either in another place as in motion or in that place as in rest according to one view) or not preceded by it, i.e., by another kawn, in the sense that it is not considered being preceded by another kawn, as in rest according to another view (and) such as (the possibility of a third intervening) between them (and its absence) in separation and conjunction. And there is no doubt that these mental considerations have no existence in the external world. (And the philosophers said: rest is the absence of motion from that which is apt to be moving) so abstract entities are not described with rest, which is a non-existent thing according to them, since they are not apt for motion. (An observation: If we say: there is nothing in the external world except al-kawn, and the distinguishing features mentioned are mental considerations, not real differentiating genera, then calling them types is metaphorical, and it is only one type) having differing attributes that do not necessitate a difference in essence (but) perhaps they also do not necessitate a difference in personal identity, for one and the same individual kawn may be 'conjunction' with respect to one part and 'separation' with respect to another part. And if we suppose an individual atom which God—exalted is He—created alone, it would not be described by conjunction or separation as long as it is solitary. (And when God—exalted is He—after that creates with it another, it occurs to it as an accidental attribute, and the kawn (is established for it or remains (in its state) its personal essence does not change, but rather its attribute).
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of al-kawn (occurrence in space) and its four types according to the mutakallimun. It begins by noting two mistaken views: some imagine a single 'occupying' subsisting in two loci, and others imagine that 'occupying' belongs to the category of position. The author then clarifies that al-kawn means 'occurrence in a space', and its existence is necessary by sense testimony. Its four types—motion, rest, conjunction, and separation—are all regarded by the mutakallimun as existing. However, upon analysis, these four types reduce to a single reality: al-kawn itself. The distinguishing features that differentiate them (e.g., being preceded by another kawn, possibility of an intervening third, etc.) are merely mental considerations (umūr iʿtibāriyya), not real differentiating genera (fuṣūl ḥaqīqiyya munawwiʿa). The author illustrates: motion is a kawn preceded by another kawn in a different place; rest is a kawn not preceded by another kawn in the same place, or with a different nuance. Conjunction and separation are distinguished by the possibility or impossibility of a third intervening. These considerations have no external existence. The philosophers hold that rest is the absence of motion in what is capable of motion, so abstract entities are not described with rest. The author then warns that if we call the four types 'types', it is metaphorical; they are actually a single type with varying attributes that do not change essence or even personal identity. The same individual kawn can be conjunction relative to one part and separation relative to another. A solitary atom would have neither conjunction nor separation. When another atom is created alongside it, it acquires the attribute (conjunction or separation) while its own essence remains unchanged.

Misconception: The four types of al-kawn are distinct real species with real differentiating genera.
Correction: The author clarifies they are merely one reality distinguished by accidental mental considerations (umūr iʿtibāriyya), not real differentiae.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun, are the four types of al-kawn (motion, rest, conjunction, separation) distinct species in reality?
A) Yes, they are distinct real species with real differentiating genera.
B) No, they are merely a single type distinguished by mental considerations.
C) They are distinct only in the philosophers' view.
D) They are distinct only in the mind, but in reality they are nonexistent.

Key Takeaway
The four types of al-kawn are not separate species but one reality with accidental mental distinctions.

Does the Inner Atom Move? The Kalam Disagreement on Body Motion
The author presents two opinions on the motion of the inner atom and traces the disagreement to the definition of space.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam scholars disagreed about the motion of the interior part of a moving body and how their disagreement hinges on the definition of space (al-hayyiz).

Core Idea
The motion of the inner atom depends on how you define its space.

This lesson explores a subtle but important disagreement in kalam physics: when a body moves, do all its parts move, or can the inner part remain at rest? The answer touches on fundamental concepts like space (al-hayyiz) and separation (al-infikak). Let's examine the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully to get an overview of the disagreement.
Pay attention to the two opinions and the concluding remark.
2
Identify the two opinions and their arguments
Identify the first opinion (middle atom moves) with its two arguments, and the second opinion (middle atom does not move) with its argument.
Notice how each side defines the space of the middle atom differently.
3
Understand the resolution
The author states that the disagreement reduces to the interpretation of al-hayyiz (space). Reflect on how the two definitions lead to different conclusions.
This is the key takeaway: the dispute is conceptual, not empirical.

بعض
قوله : ( ولأنه أي الجوهر المتوسط إلخ) ليس من قبيل قياس المساواة كما ترى من ظاهره حتى يعترض عليه بأن الدخول للمحاط في المحيط بل مآله إلى الشكل الأول هو أن الجوهر المتوسط داخل في الشيء الحاصل في الحيز وكل ما هو داخل في الشيء الحاصل في الحيز حاصل في ذلك الحيز فالجوهر المتوسط حاصل في ذلك الحيز . قوله فالاختلاف راجع إلى تفسير (الحيز) لا يخفى أنه بعدما تقرر أن الحيز عند
:قوله : فاتفقوا على حركة الجواهر (الظاهرة أراد به اتفاق الجمهور وهم القائلون بأن جالس السفينة متحرك لاتفاق الكل إذ القائلون بسكون الجالس في السفينة المتحركة بناء على أن الحيز ما اعتمد عليه ثقل الجوهر قائلون بسكون الجواهر الظاهرة أيضاً كما لا يخفى. قوله : ( فلا يكون متحركاً) وحديث لزوم الانفكاك على هذا التوجيه بين البطلان ولذا لم
يتعرض له.
The fourth observation - the fourth purpose: the disagreement on the motion of al-kawn. [The fourth purpose: the disagreement on the motion of al-kawn] Concerning what they disagreed about in its being moving: that is in two cases. The first: when a body moves from one place to another, they agreed on the motion of the apparent atoms (jawahir) of it because they have left their spaces (ahyaz), and they disagreed about the interior middle atom (al-jawhar al-mutawassit al-batin) of it. One (faction) said: it is moving, otherwise it would be at rest, for there is no intermediate between them (motion and rest) in what is receptive to them after the first time of its occurrence, and it is not at rest. For if it were at rest with the motion of the remaining parts, separation (infikak) would necessarily follow, and the disconnection of some parts from others, and what is perceived by sense is contrary to that. And because that middle atom is inside the whole, and the whole is inside the space of the whole, so it is inside the space of the whole, thus it is also located (mutahayyiz) by it. And the middle atom has departed from it (the space of the whole) to another space, since it is supposed that the whole has entirely left its space, so it (the middle atom) is also moving. And it was said: the middle atom is not moving, since its space is the surrounding atoms, and it has not departed from them nor separated from them, so it is stable in its space; it is moving? (unclear). And the first group (who say it is moving) made its al-hayyiz (space) to be the supposed dimension (bu'd mafrud) that the middle atom occupies, which is a part of the space of the whole, and there is no doubt that it has left it, so it is moving. Thus the disagreement reduces to the interpretation of al-hayyiz (space).
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents a disagreement in kalam physics about the motion of the inner parts of a moving body. The author states that when a body moves from one place to another, scholars agree that the apparent (outer) atoms are moving because they leave their spaces. However, they disagree about the interior middle atom (al-jawhar al-mutawassit al-batin). One view holds that it is also moving, otherwise it would be at rest; but if it were at rest while the outer parts move, separation (infikak) would occur, contradicting sensory perception. Additionally, the middle atom is part of the whole, and since the whole moves, each part moves. The second view holds that the middle atom is not moving because its space is the surrounding atoms, and it has not left them. The first group counters that the middle atom's space is a supposed dimension (bu'd mafrud) that it occupies, which is a part of the whole's space, and since the whole leaves its space, the middle atom also leaves that part. The author concludes that the disagreement reduces to a different interpretation of what al-hayyiz (space) is. The marginal glosses further clarify: the agreement on outer atoms is the position of the majority (including those who say a passenger on a moving ship is moving), and the argument based on 'the middle atom is inside the whole' is actually a first-figure syllogism, not a proportional analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The motion of the inner atom can be determined by direct observation.
Correction: The debate is conceptual and relies on logical inference and definitions of space, not empirical observation.

Checkpoint
What is the crux of the disagreement regarding the motion of the inner atom in a moving body?
A) Whether the inner atom can be seen moving.
B) Whether the inner atom is a substance or an accident.
C) The interpretation of what constitutes al-hayyiz (space) for the inner atom.
D) Whether the body moves as a whole or not.

Key Takeaway
The disagreement on the motion of the inner atom in a moving body ultimately depends on how one defines the space (al-hayyiz) of that atom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that a key term like 'space' can be defined differently, leading to opposite conclusions. In your own life, what definition do you hold for something you consider fundamental (e.g., 'success', 'truth', 'justice')? Could a different definition change your actions or beliefs? Will you examine one such term today to see if it truly aligns with the evidence?
?
The scholars relied on logical inference to determine the motion of an unobservable atom. Are there aspects of your faith that you accept based on inference rather than direct experience? How confident are you in those inferences? Take a moment to trace the reasoning behind one of your core beliefs and see if it holds up under scrutiny.
?
The inner atom's motion is tied to the whole body's coherence—if it were still, the body would fall apart. Reflect on your own 'inner atom'—your heart or intentions. When your outer actions change, does your inner state move with them or stay fixed? How does that affect your spiritual integrity?
